The Dilemma of the Eternal Source of Evil
Baqillani poses a dilemma to refute the Magian claim about the source of evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma used by Imam Baqillani to refute the Magian claim that the source of evil is eternal.

Core Idea
If the source of evil is eternal, the devil must be eternal too.

The Magians argued that evil (the devil) originated from an eternal doubt or thought in God. Imam Baqillani responds with a sharp dilemma: if the source of evil is eternal, then the devil must also be eternal, contradicting their claim that the devil is created. Additionally, an eternal doubt would imply eternal ignorance in God, which is impossible. This lesson walks through that powerful refutation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Magian claim
The Magians believe that evil (the devil) originated from an eternal doubt or thought in God. They consider the source eternal but the devil itself created.
This claim is the target of Baqillani's refutation.
2
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage slowly, then read the translation. Notice the question at the beginning and the two consequences given.
The Arabic uses the terms 'qadīm' (eternal) and 'muḥdath' (originated).
3
Analyze the dilemma
Identify the two consequences of saying the source is eternal: (1) It would make God ignorant, and (2) The devil would also be eternal. Both contradict Magian beliefs.
The argument is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
4
See the inescapability
Note the conclusion: the Magians have no escape. If they accept the source as eternal, they must give up the temporality of the devil. If they reject, the source is originated, contradicting their initial claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مَسْأَلَةٌ فِي نَقْضِ الْمَجُوسِ. ثُمَّ يُقَالُ لَهُمْ: خَبِّرُونَا عَنِ الشَّكِّ أَوِ التَّفَكُّرِ أَوِ الْعُقُوبَةِ الَّتِي حَدَثَ مِنْهَا الشَّيْطَانُ، أَمُحْدَثٌ ذَلِكَ أَمْ قَدِيمٌ؟ فَإِنْ قَالُوا بِقِدَمِ هَذِهِ الْأُمُورِ، أَلْزَمُوا إِحَالَةَ كَوْنِ الْبَارِي عَالِماً وَأَوْجَبَ عَلَيْهِمْ قِدَمَ الْجَهْلِ. ثُمَّ قِيلَ لَهُمْ: فَإِنْ كَانَ الشَّكُّ وَالْفِكْرُ وَالْعُقُوبَةُ الَّتِي عَنْهَا كَانَ الشَّيْطَانُ قَدِيمَةً، فَمَا أَنْكَرْتُمْ أَنْ يَكُونَ الشَّيْطَانُ قَدِيماً لِقِدَمِ مَا كَانَ عَنْهُ؟ فَإِنْ مَرُّوا عَلَى ذَلِكَ تَرَكُوا قَوْلَهُمْ بِحُدُوثِهِ، وَلَا خَلَاصَ لَهُمْ مِنْ ذَلِكَ.
An issue concerning the refutation of the Magians. Then it is said to them: Inform us about the doubt or thinking or punishment from which the devil occurred — is that originated (muḥdath) or eternal (qadīm)? If they say that these things are eternal, then they are compelled to the impossibility of the Creator being knowing, and they are obliged to affirm the eternity of ignorance. Then it is said to them: If the doubt, thought, and punishment from which the devil was are eternal, then what prevents you from saying that the devil is eternal because of the eternity of that from which he came? For if they assent to that, they abandon their statement about his temporality (ḥudūth), and there is no escape for them from that.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical refutation of the Magian claim that evil (the devil) originated from a doubt or thought in God. The author presents a dilemma: the source (doubt, thought, or punishment) that produced the devil — is it originated (muḥdath) or eternal (qadīm)? If the Magians say it is eternal, then two consequences follow: (1) It would make it impossible for the Creator to be knowing (because an eternal doubt would mean eternal ignorance), and (2) If the source is eternal, then its effect (the devil) should also be eternal, because an eternal cause necessitates an eternal effect. But the Magians affirm that the devil is originated (ḥādith). So they would have to give up that claim. Thus, they have no escape: they cannot consistently maintain both that the source is eternal and the devil is originated. The argument is structured as a question, leading the opponent to an unacceptable conclusion.

Key Terms
مُحْدَثٌ (originated)
قَدِيمٌ (eternal)
الْجَهْلُ (ignorance)
حُدُوثٌ (temporality)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the devil's temporality is independent of its source; that the source can be eternal while the effect is temporal.
Correction: The passage shows that an eternal cause necessitates an eternal effect. If the source is eternal, the devil must also be eternal.

Checkpoint
What is the first consequence if the Magians claim that the doubt/thought/punishment is eternal?
A) The devil becomes eternal as well.
B) They must affirm the eternity of ignorance.
C) They must deny the existence of the devil.
D) They must say God is ignorant.

Key Takeaway
The devil's temporality disproves the eternity of its source; an eternal cause necessitates an eternal effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument shows that if the source of evil were eternal, God would not be all-knowing. Do you ever act as if something in your life—a struggle, a failure, a sin—has an origin beyond God's knowledge and control? What would change in your relationship with Allah if you truly internalized that nothing can be eternal except Him?
?
The Magians tried to shift the origin of evil away from God. In your own heart, do you sometimes blame circumstances, other people, or even the devil for your wrong choices as if they have an independent power? How does this argument call you to take full responsibility for your actions and to repent?
?
If you truly believed that every originated thing—including your doubts and thoughts—comes from a temporal source within God's creative power, would you still despair over your shortcomings? What would it mean for you to trust that even your failures are under God's wise decree?

Story of Two Children: Refuting Mu'tazilite Views on Divine Justice
Baqillani relates a story criticizing Mu'tazilite views on children's fate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses a hypothetical story to expose the logical inconsistency of Mu'tazilite views on childhood and divine justice.

Core Idea
A hypothetical story reveals the absurdity of Mu'tazilite qadar doctrine.

In this lesson, we encounter a powerful narrative employed by Imam Baqillani to critique a Mu'tazilite position on the fate of children. The story presents a dialogue that forces the Mu'tazilite framework into a logical trap, demonstrating its internal incoherence. As you read, notice how the argument unfolds through the voices of the characters and how the author condemns the doctrine at the end.

Guided Path
1
Read the story carefully
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation. Let the narrative unfold without analysis. Notice the sequence of dialogues.
This story is a reductio ad absurdum, not a literal event.
2
Identify the theological problem in each exchange
After reading, reflect on each character's question and God's answer. Consider what flaw in Mu'tazilite thought each exchange highlights.
The child who died young asks about rank, then about not reaching puberty. The person in Hell then objects.
3
Examine the author's condemnation
Read the final sentence where Baqillani seeks refuge from such a doctrine. Understand how the story serves as a refutation.
The story is a rhetorical device to show the doctrine leads to absurd consequences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطفولية وبلغ منهم اثنان فكفر أحدهما، وآمن آخر فيدخل الله يوم القيامة في الجنة من مات في حال الطفولية ولا يبلغه منها الدرجة العظيمة، ويدخل الذي آمن الجنة ويعطيه الدرجة العظيمة ويدخل الذي كفر النار. فيقول الطفل الذي مات في صغره: لم لم تبلغني درجة الذي آمن بعد البلوغ؟ فيقول له: لأنه آمن وأنت لم تؤمن. فيقول الذي مات طفلاً: هلا بلغتني حال البلوغ حتى كنت أؤمن بك كما آمن هو؟ فيقول الله تعالى له: لم أبلغك حال البلوغ لأني علمت أنك لو بقيت لكفرت فاخترمتك قبل البلوغ لأن صلاحك كان فيه حتى سلمت من النار. سمع الذي في النار هذا الكلام يقول: فلم لم تختر مني قبل البلوغ حتى كنت أسلم النار وكان يكون فيه صلاحي فنعوذ بالله من مذهب يؤدي إلى مثل هذه الرذيلة.
Childhood, and two of them reached it: one disbelieved, and another believed. So on the Day of Resurrection, God enters into Paradise the one who died in childhood, but does not grant him the great rank from it; and He enters the one who believed into Paradise and gives him the great rank; and enters the one who disbelieved into the Fire. Then the child who died young says: 'Why did you not grant me the rank of the one who believed after puberty?' He is told: 'Because he believed and you did not believe.' Then the one who died as a child says: 'Why did you not let me reach puberty so that I could believe in you as he believed?' Then God, exalted, says to him: 'I did not let you reach puberty because I knew that if you remained, you would disbelieve, so I took you before puberty because your well-being was in that, so that you would be saved from the Fire.' The one in the Fire hears this speech and says: 'Why did you not take me before puberty so that I could be saved from the Fire, and my well-being would be in that?' So we seek refuge with God from a doctrine that leads to such vileness.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage narrates a hypothetical scenario to expose the absurdity of a certain Mu'tazilite doctrine. Two children reach puberty: one becomes a believer, one a disbeliever. A child who died young questions why he did not get the rank of the believer. God explains that He knew if he lived he would disbelieve, so He took him early to save him. Then a person in Hell argues why he was not taken early. The author condemns the doctrine that leads to such inconsistencies, using the story as a refutation.

Key Terms
الطفولية
البلوغ
الدرجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The story is a literal conversation that happened between God and individuals on the Day of Judgment.
Correction: This is a hypothetical scenario (reductio ad absurdum) used to expose the logical flaws of the Mu'tazilite doctrine, not a report of an actual event.

Checkpoint
According to the story, why does God tell the child that he was not allowed to reach puberty?
A) Because he was sinful.
B) Because God knew he would disbelieve if he lived.
C) Because he was not predestined for faith.
D) Because he preferred not to.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani uses a story to argue that Mu'tazilite doctrine leads to absurd questions about divine justice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This story shows God acting based on His knowledge of what is best for each person, even if it seems unfair to us. Is there a situation in your life where you have questioned God's wisdom because it didn't match your sense of fairness? Will you trust His knowledge over your own feelings starting today?
?
The child in the story objects to his fate, and the person in Hell argues why he wasn't given the same mercy. When you face trials or missed opportunities, do you find yourself complaining to God, expecting Him to justify His actions? How can you shift from demanding answers to submitting to His decree?
?
Baqillani seeks refuge from a doctrine that leads to such vile questions. Are there any beliefs you hold about God that, if followed to their logical conclusion, would lead to questioning His justice or wisdom? What step can you take today to purify your understanding of God's attributes?

Refuting the Objection That Uncommanded Actions Imply Incapacity
Baqillani refutes the objection by citing God's own uncommanded actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani refutes the argument that uncommanded actions imply God's incapacity by appealing to God's own uncommanded actions.

Core Idea
God's own uncommanded actions prove that uncommanded doesn't mean inability.

This passage presents a key objection in the Ash'ari theology of divine attributes. The objector claims that if God creates actions He did not command, that suggests He is incapable of preventing them. Baqillani responds with a powerful analogy: many of God's own actions were not commanded by anyone, yet He is not incapable. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the objection and refutation.
Notice that the Arabic ends mid-sentence at a page break, but the logical point is complete.
2
Identify the objection and refutation
Find the objector's claim (lines 1-2) and Baqillani's response (lines 2-4). Note how he uses God's own actions as a counterexample.
3
Reflect on the analogy
Ask yourself: Why is the comparison between God's actions and creatures' actions valid here? What does it reveal about divine power and command?
This connects to the broader Ash'ari position that God's power is absolute and independent of any external command.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: فيجب إذا كان من خلقه ما لم يأمرهم به، أن يدل ذلك على عجزه. قيل لهم: هذا ساقط باتفاق، لأنه قد كانت أكثر أفعاله ولم يأمر بها أحداً خلقه، ولم يلحقه العجز فكذلك حكم فعل خلقه له. ولأنه، إذا
If they say: Then it would be necessary, if there is from His creation what He did not command them to do, that that indicates His incapacity. Say to them: This is invalid by agreement, because there have been many of His actions that He did not command anyone of His creation to do, and incapacity does not attach to Him, so similarly is the ruling of the action of His creation for Him. And because, if
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its refutation. The objector argues: If God creates actions in His servants that He did not command (such as disbelief), that should indicate incapacity, because the command implies ability to prevent. The refutation: This is invalid by consensus, because many of God's own actions (e.g., creating the universe, bringing rain) were not commanded by anyone, yet God is not incapable. Therefore, the lack of command does not imply incapacity in the case of creatures' actions either. The argument appears cut off but the logical point is complete: command is not a condition for power; God's power is absolute and independent of any external command.

Key Terms
أمر
عجز
فعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that if God does not command an action, then He must be incapable of preventing it (conflating moral command with creative will).
Correction: God's own uncommanded actions (like creation itself) show that lack of command does not imply lack of power, since no one commands God. Similarly, His creating uncommanded actions in creatures does not imply incapacity.

Checkpoint
How does Baqillani refute the claim that uncommanded actions imply God's incapacity?
A) By stating that God's actions are not subject to human judgment.
B) By pointing out that many of God's own actions were not commanded by anyone.
C) By denying that humans have free will.
D) By citing a verse from the Quran.

Key Takeaway
God's uncommanded actions do not imply incapacity, so the same applies to the actions of His creatures.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you genuinely trust that God's lack of command over certain events in your life does not mean He has lost control? How does this truth change your response to trials you did not choose?
?
When you fail to obey a command of God, do you subconsciously think that your disobedience limits His power over you? How does this lesson correct that assumption?
?
Why do you often feel that if God did not command a specific outcome, it must be outside His will? How can you train your heart to rest in His absolute power regardless of your actions?

Refuting the Polytheists' Mocking Excuse
Baqillani interprets the polytheists' statement as mockery and refutes it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the polytheists' statement 'If Allah had willed, we would not have associated partners' was not a genuine theological argument but a form of mockery, and how Baqillani refutes it by drawing parallels to the hypocrites.

Core Idea
The polytheists' excuse of divine will was mockery, not belief.

In this passage, Baqillani unpacks a key Quranic verse often misused by polytheists. He shows that their words were not a sincere expression of qadar but a jesting rejection of the messengers. By drawing parallels to the hypocrites, he exposes the insincerity behind such excuses and teaches us to discern genuine theological statements from mocking pretexts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Baqillani uses a very interpretive style here.
2
Identify the polytheists' statement and Baqillani's interpretation
The polytheists said, 'If Allah had willed, we would not have associated partners.' Baqillani explains they said it in jest and mockery. Find where this interpretation is given.
The phrase 'على جهة الهزل والاستهزاء' is key.
3
Understand the parallel to contemporary arguments
Baqillani compares the polytheists' mockery to people today who say, 'If Allah had willed, we would have believed,' but say it mockingly while rejecting divine decree. Reflect on this analogy.
4
Analyze the hypocrite analogy
Baqillani compares the polytheists to the hypocrites who testified to the Prophet's prophethood insincerely. See how this strengthens the refutation.
The verse about the hypocrites (Quran 63:1) is cited.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أشركوا لو شاء الله ما أشركنا ولا آباؤنا الآية؟ قيل لهم: معنى ذلك أنهم قالوا ذلك على جهة الهزل والاستهزاء بالرسل والمؤمنين في قولهم: ولو شاء الله لهداكم، ولو شاء لأمنتم. فقالوا في جواب هذا مستهزئين: ولو شاء الله ما أشركنا ولا آباؤنا، منكرين بذلك لما قاله الرسل والمؤمنون. وهذا جواب على حد ما تقولونه أنتم أبدا لنا: لو شاء الله أن نؤمن لآمنا، ولو قدر وقضى لنا الطاعة لأطعنا، مستهزئين بذلك ومنكرين لقضاء الله وقدره ومشيئته. وعلى هذا النحو جاء قوله في إنكاره على المنافقين في قولهم للنبي صلى الله عليه وسلم: إنك لرسول الله حيث قال: إذا جاءك المنافقون قالوا نشهد إنك لرسول الله والله يعلم إنك لرسوله والله يشهد إن المنافقين لكاذبون. فأنكر الله ذلك عليهم لكونهم غير معتقدين لصحة ما قالوه، وكما أنكر على من قال: أنطعم من لو يشاء الله أطعمه، لقولهم هذا على سبيل الاستهزاء والهزل بالرسل لما أخبروهم أن الله تعالى لو شاء أن يطعمهم لأطعمهم.
Those who associated partners [said]: 'If Allah had willed, we would not have associated partners, nor our fathers' – the verse. It was said to them: The meaning of that is that they said that in the manner of jest and mockery of the messengers and the believers in their saying: 'And if Allah had willed, He would have guided you, and if He had willed, you would have believed.' So they said in response to this, mocking: 'If Allah had willed, we would not have associated partners, nor our fathers,' denying thereby what the messengers and believers said. And this is a response similar to what you yourselves always say to us: 'If Allah had willed that we believe, we would have believed,' and 'If He had decreed and ordained obedience for us, we would have obeyed,' mocking thereby and denying Allah's decree and predestination and will. And in this manner came His saying in His denial of the hypocrites in their saying to the Prophet, peace be upon him: 'Indeed you are the Messenger of Allah,' where He said: 'When the hypocrites come to you, they say: We testify that you are indeed the Messenger of Allah, and Allah knows that you are indeed His Messenger, and Allah testifies that the hypocrites are indeed liars.' So Allah denied that upon them because they were not believing in the truth of what they said, and just as He denied upon those who said: 'Shall we feed those whom, if Allah willed, He would have fed?' because they said this in the way of mockery and jest with the messengers when they informed them that Allah Almighty, if He willed, would have fed them.
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage presents an interpretation and refutation of the polytheists' argument. Baqillani begins by quoting the polytheists' statement from the Quran: 'If Allah had willed, we would not have associated partners, nor our fathers.' He then explains that this statement was not a genuine theological claim but was uttered in jest (hazl) and mockery (istihza') of the messengers and believers. The messengers had told them that if Allah willed, He would guide them. The polytheists responded mockingly with their verse, denying the messengers' assertion. Baqillani draws a parallel to a contemporary argument: people often say, 'If Allah willed, we would have believed' or 'If He decreed obedience, we would have obeyed,' but they say this mockingly while actually rejecting divine decree. He then supports his interpretation with the example of the hypocrites who verbally testified to the Prophet's prophethood but inwardly disbelieved; Allah called them liars because they did not genuinely believe in their words. Similarly, those who use the excuse of divine will as a pretext for abandoning obedience are not sincere. Thus, the polytheists' statement is not a valid theological objection but a form of mockery.

Key Terms
الهزل (hazl, jest)
الاستهزاء (istihza', mockery)
المنافقون (munafiqun, hypocrites)
القضاء والقدر (al-qada' wa al-qadr, divine decree and predestination)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think the polytheists' statement 'If Allah had willed...' was a sincere theological argument based on predestination.
Correction: Baqillani shows that it was said mockingly, not as a genuine belief, and is therefore invalid as an excuse.

Checkpoint
Why does Baqillani consider the polytheists' statement 'If Allah willed, we would not have associated partners' invalid?
A) Because it contradicts the Quran
B) Because they said it in jest and mockery, not as a genuine belief
C) Because they were unaware of Allah's will
D) Because they were compelled by predestination

Key Takeaway
The polytheists used divine will as a mocking excuse, which Baqillani refutes by showing their insincerity and drawing a parallel to the hypocrites.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When in your own life have you used the excuse 'if Allah willed' to avoid obeying a clear command? What does that reveal about the sincerity of your faith, and what specific step will you take today to replace excuse with action?
?
Are there moments when you have mocked sincere believers in your heart, even if outwardly you remained silent? How can you purify your heart from such mockery and truly honor those who strive in Allah's path?
?
The polytheists used divine will as a shield for their disobedience. Examine your own avoidance of obligations: are you hiding behind divine decree to justify laziness or sin? What is one thing you will commit to changing starting now, relying on Allah's help rather than making excuses?

Scriptural Evidence for Two Capabilities
Baqillani cites Quranic verses to prove two types of capability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses Quranic verses to distinguish between the capability of means/states and the capability of the act, and how he proves the existence of a power of states.

Core Idea
Quranic verses reveal two distinct types of capability, not just one.

Imam Baqillani now turns to scriptural evidence to support the division of capability into two kinds. The Quran itself uses the term 'istita'ah' in ways that point to either the capability of means and circumstances or the capability linked to the act itself. This lesson examines the verses he cites and the reasoning behind his classification.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation to get an overview of the argument. Notice the three sets of verses cited.
2
Identify the two capabilities
Baqillani distinguishes between capability of means/states and capability of the act. The first evidence (faman lam yastati', hypocrites) points to capability of means. The second (Moses and Khidr, inability to hear) points to capability of states.
Capability of means includes factors like health, wealth, and circumstances.
3
Analyze the first set of verses
Examine the verse about expiation and the hypocrites' excuse. Baqillani argues that these refer to capability of existence (ability to fast or have means), not the direct power to perform the act.
The hypocrites claimed inability due to illness or lack of wealth, not lack of power to act.
4
Analyze the second set of verses
Look at the verses about 'not able to hear' and the story of Moses and Khidr. Baqillani interprets these as confirming a power of states, negated when actions cease.
The repetition in the Moses-Khidr story emphasizes the type of capability involved.
5
Reflect on the incomplete ending
The passage ends abruptly with 'then the evidence for the obligation of the burden without...' This suggests the argument continues. Reflect on how the two types of capability relate to obligation.
This might connect to the concept of taklif (moral responsibility).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Then the evidence for the division of the two capabilities (istita'atayn) is the saying of Allah Almighty: 'And whoever cannot [fast], then a feeding of sixty poor people.' And the saying of others who said, 'If we had the capability, we would have gone out with you.' Then the evidence that capability is the capability of means and states, not the capability of the act, has several aspects. One of them is that His saying 'Whoever cannot' refers to fasting for two months, and no one knows that the power of the act is not repelled by that period; thus it is established that what is meant by that is the capability of existence. Similarly, the hypocrites did not know the capability by which actions [occur]; rather they meant by that illness or loss of wealth as Allah Almighty explained in His saying: 'There is no blame upon the weak' up to His saying: 'The way [of blame] is only against those who ask permission of you while they are rich.' As for the evidence for the other capability, it is His saying: 'They were not able to hear' and the saying of the companion of Moses: 'Indeed, you will not be able to have patience with me.' Then he said: 'Did I not tell you that you would not be able to have patience with me?' Then he said: 'That is the interpretation of that about which you could not have patience.' This confirms the power of states; He negated it when the actions ceased. Likewise His saying: 'So fear Allah as much as you are able' and other than that. Then the evidence for the obligation of the burden without
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage presents the scriptural evidence for the division of capability into two types. The author quotes verses showing that 'capability' in the Quran refers to means and conditions, not the direct power to perform an act. For example, the verse about expiation for breaking the fast (*faman lam yastati'*) is interpreted as capability of existence (e.g., being able to fast a substitute period). The hypocrites' claim of inability is shown to refer to illness or lack of wealth. For the second type, verses like 'they were not able to hear' and the story of Moses and Khidr show a capability that is negated when the action ceases, confirming it is a power of states. The passage concludes by hinting at the evidence for the necessity of the burden (al-kulfa).

Correction: Baqillani shows that some verses refer to capability of means/states, while others refer to a power of states that is tied to actions.

Baqillani demonstrates that Quranic usage of 'capability' supports two distinct realities: capability of means/states and capability of the act (or power of states).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you tell yourself 'I can't' in an act of worship—like praying at night or fasting—are you truly incapable of the act itself, or are you lacking the means (time, energy, discipline)? How often do you hide behind the excuse of 'no capability' when the real issue is your reluctance?
?
The hypocrites claimed they lacked the ability to join the expedition, but Allah exposed their lie: they had the power of act but lacked the will. In your life, are there commands of Allah that you have the physical ability to perform but choose not to, convincing yourself it's beyond your capacity?
?
The Quran says 'Fear Allah as much as you are able' (64:16). This lesson teaches that your 'ability' includes both means and will. What is one thing today that you have the means and power to do for Allah, but have been avoiding? Commit to doing it starting today.

Proof of Human Capability from Self-Awareness
Baqillani argues for human capability based on experiential distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses internal self-awareness to argue for human capability (kasb) as distinct from compulsion.

Core Idea
We know we can act by choice because we feel the difference between voluntary and involuntary actions.

This passage presents a key argument in the Ash'ari doctrine of kasb. Baqillani responds to an objection by pointing to a universal human experience: everyone knows the difference between actions they do by choice and actions forced upon them. This experiential distinction becomes the foundation for affirming that humans have a genuine role in their actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Note the structure: an objection and its answer.
The passage is concise; pay attention to the list of negated explanations.
2
Identify the objection and the answer
The passage begins with a concession ('Yes') and a question. The answer starts with 'because man knows within himself a difference'. Pinpoint exactly what is being objected and how the author responds.
3
Examine the elimination of alternatives
The author lists four things that do NOT explain the difference between voluntary and involuntary acts (genus, knowledge, location, intention). Ask yourself why each is dismissed.
This is a key move to isolate the real cause.
4
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion states that the voluntary act must be accompanied by the person's acquisition (kasb) because he is capable of it. Reflect on how the previous steps lead to this.
This conclusion affirms human responsibility without denying divine power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل له : أجل . فإن قال : ولم قلتم ذلك؟ قلنا لأن الإنسان يعرف من نفسه فرقاً بين قيامه وقعوده وكلامه إذا كان واقعاً بحسب اختياره وقصده، وبين ما يُضطر إليه مما لا قدرة له عليه من الزمانة والمرض والحركة من الفالج وغير ذلك . وليس يفترق الشيئان في ذلك الجنسهما، ولا للعلم بهما، ولا لاختلاف محلّهما، ولا للإرادة لأحدهما . فوجب أن يحصل مع كسبه على هذه الصفة لكونه قادرا عليه .
It was said to him: Yes. Then he said: And why do you say that? We said: Because man knows within himself a difference between his standing and sitting and speaking when it occurs according to his choice and intention, and between that to which he is compelled which he has no power over, such as chronic disease, illness, and movement from paralysis and other than that. And the two things are not distinguished in that by their genus, nor by knowledge of them, nor by difference of their locus, nor by intention for one of them. So it is necessary that he has with his acquisition (kasb) this description because he is capable of it.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer argument for human capability. The interlocutor concedes a previous point and asks for the reasoning. The answer uses self-awareness: man perceives a clear difference between voluntary actions (like standing by choice) and involuntary ones (like trembling from illness). This difference is not based on genus, knowledge, location, or intention. Therefore, the voluntary actions must be attributed to the person's own acquisition (kasb) because he is capable of them. The argument relies on internal experience rather than external evidence.

Correction: Baqillani is affirming human capability (kasb) as a genuine acquisition, not denying divine creation. The argument establishes that humans have a role in their actions, not that they create them independently.

Acquisition Is by a Power That Occurs to the Human
Baqillani answers whether acquisition is by the self or a power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that human acquisition is not by an inherent self-power but by a power that occurs to the human, and the evidence from the variability of capability.

Core Idea
Human power to act is not inherent; it is a created power that occurs to them.

Imam Baqillani addresses a key question in kalam: does the human have an independent ability to acquire actions, or is the ability a gift that comes and goes? In this brief passage, he gives a clear answer and a simple but powerful proof from the variability of human capability.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the question-and-answer format.
2
Identify the question and answer
Find what the questioner asks and how Baqillani responds. The answer is a denial followed by a positive claim.
The key phrase is 'by a power that occurs to him'.
3
Examine the evidence
The evidence is the variability of capability: a person can move at one time but not at another. Why does this support the answer?
If power were inherent, it would always be present. Its absence shows it is not inherent.
4
Reflect on the theological implication
Think about how this relates to Qadar: all power to act is created by Allah and given to humans momentarily.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال: فهل تزعمون أنه يستطيع أن يكتسب بنفسه، أو بقدرة؟ قلنا: لا، بل بقدرة تحدث له والدليل على ذلك كونه قادراً على الحركة مرة، وغير قادر عليها أخرى، وعلى ما هو مثلها ومن جنسها مرة أخرى.
Then he said: Do you claim that he is able to acquire by himself, or by a power? We said: No, but by a power that occurs to him. The evidence for that is that he is capable of movement at one time and not capable of it at another time, and of what is similar to it and of its genus at another time.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage is a short objection-and-answer. The questioner asks whether human acquisition (kasb) is done by the human himself or by a power. The answer is that it is not by himself, but by a power that occurs to him (haditha). The evidence is the variability of capability: a person is capable of movement at one moment and incapable at another, even for similar actions. This indicates that capability is not inherent but is a power that comes and goes.

Obligation Based on Means, Not Act-Power
Baqillani explains that obligation depends on capability of means, not act-power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani argues that religious obligation depends on the availability of means (istita'ah al-sabil) rather than on the actual power to perform the act at the moment of obligation.

Core Idea
Obligation follows means, not act-power. Means make duty real.

Baqillani addresses a key theological question: When is a person obligated? Is it when they have the power to do the act, or when they have the means? This passage argues for the latter, using clear scriptural and rational evidence. Let us examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Baqillani begins with an objection, then answers.
2
Identify the objection and answer structure
The passage starts with a hypothetical question about capability before or during acquisition. Find how Baqillani responds, dividing his answer into revelation and reason.
3
Examine the key evidence: pilgrimage verse
Pay special attention to the verse about Hajj: 'whoever is able to find a way to it.' Notice how Baqillani uses it to show that obligation is tied to having means (provisions, mount), not to having the power to perform the act at that moment.
This is the cornerstone of his argument.
4
Follow the rational argument
Baqillani explains that if obligation required the actual power to act, pilgrimage would never be obligatory until the power to travel arises, which would lead to absurd consequences. Identify the logical chain.
5
Understand the conclusion and its implications
The passage ends by stating that obligation is based on states (ahwal), not on the power of the act itself. Consider how this applies to all acts of worship.
This is the main takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال: فهل تزعمون أنه يستطيع الفعل قبل اكتسابه، أو في حال اكتسابه؟ . حقيقة هذا النوع من القدرة والسمع والعقل، فأما السمع فما أخبرت من الآيات على نفي الاستطاعة، ثم الأمر والنهي والتعيير على ذلك وإدراك العقل، ثم الذي يوضح هذا أيضاً قوله تعالى: ﴿ والله على الناس حج البيت من استطاع إليه سبيلا ) ومعلوم أنه لاسبيل إلى حقيقة الأفعال حتى يجد الزاد والراحلة، ولو كان لا يجب إلا بوجود حقيقة القدرة قدرة الفعل لم يكن ليلزم أحداً ذلك، إذ قدرة الأفعال هي التي تحدث على حدوث الأوقات والحج غير واجب حتى ترد هي، وهي لا ترد إلا بقطع الأسفار فيكون له التخلف إذ هو غير واجب، وكذلك أمر الجهاد، إذ لو علم أن الذي معه من قوة الأسباب لا يبلغه لم يعرض عليه الخروج، ومعلوم أن قوة العقل بعد البلوغ ليست معه للحال، وقد لزمه فرضه حيث عير من قعد. وكذلك نجد القيام والصيام ونحو ذلك يكون له الخروج من ذلك بالبدل، وإن كان قدرة حقيقة الفعل قد توجد بالجهد، ثبت أن فرض الأشياء ليس بها، ولكن بالأحوال. وعلى ذلك جميع العبادات ، من يعلم أن ليس معه السبب ما يتم به الصلاة أو الصيام أو الحج لم يكلف ابتداء ذلك.
Then he said: Do you claim that he is capable of the act before his acquisition, or at the time of his acquisition? [Answer:] The reality of this type of power is [known] through revelation and reason. As for revelation, it is what I have reported of the verses negating capability, then command and prohibition and censure upon that, and the perception of reason. Then what clarifies this also is His saying: 'And due to Allah from the people is pilgrimage to the House for whoever is able to find a way to it.' And it is known that there is no way to the reality of actions until one finds provisions and mount. And if it were not obligatory except with the existence of the reality of power, the power of the act, then it would not be binding on anyone, because the power of acts is what occurs at the occurrence of times, and pilgrimage is not obligatory until it [the power] occurs, and it does not occur except by traversing journeys, so he would have the option to stay behind since it is not obligatory. Similarly, the matter of jihad, for if one knew that what he has of strength of means would not suffice him, he would not be asked to go out. And it is known that the strength of reason after puberty is not with him at the moment, yet the obligation has been imposed on him where the one who sat back was censured. Similarly, we find standing, fasting, and the like: he can get out of them by substitution, even though the power of the reality of the act may exist with effort. It is thus established that the obligation of things is not by it [the power of the act], but by states (ahwal). And upon this are all acts of worship. Whoever knows that he does not have the means to complete the prayer, fasting, or pilgrimage, he is not initially obligated.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer about the timing of capability: is it before acquisition or during? The author answers using both revelation and reason. Revelation provides verses that negate capability in some contexts, but also command and censure. The key evidence is the pilgrimage verse: obligation depends on having the means (way, provisions, mount). If obligation required the actual power to perform the act at that moment, no one would be obligated until that power occurs—but pilgrimage becomes obligatory when one has the means, even if the power to travel comes later. Similarly, jihad and other acts show that obligation is based on states (ahwal) and means, not on the power of the act itself. Even the ability to substitute (e.g., standing for prayer) shows that obligation is not tied to the act-power. The conclusion: all obligations are based on capability of means and states.

قدرة الأحوال
فرض
تكليف
استطاعة السبيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One is only obligated if one already has the power to perform the act at that moment.
Correction: Baqillani shows that obligation is based on the availability of means, not on the power to complete the act. For example, Hajj is obligatory once one has the means (provisions, mount), even though the power to travel may not yet be present.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, on what is religious obligation (taklif) based?
A) The power to perform the act at that moment
B) The capability of means and states (ahwal)

D) The presence of angels

Key Takeaway
Religious obligation depends on having the means to perform the act, not on possessing the power to do it at that moment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Meaning of Capability in the Maintenance Verse
Answer clarifying that maintenance verse does not affirm capability before action

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the verse on wives' maintenance does not prove that capability must exist before action, but rather refers to actual availability.

Core Idea
The maintenance verse addresses obligation, not pre-existing capability.

In this short passage, Imam Baqillani responds to an objection about a verse concerning the financial maintenance of wives. Some might think the verse implies that capability (istita'a) must be present before the act is obligated. The Imam clarifies that the verse only means God does not oblige what is impossible, not that capability pre-exists the act.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation to understand the plain meaning.
Notice the negation at the end of the passage.
2
Identify the two parts of the clarification
The author first states what the verse means (obligation only for what is found and possible), then states what it does not mean (affirmation of capability before action).
3
Understand the theological implication
This refutes the view that capability must pre-exist obligation. Instead, capability is contemporaneous with or after the act in this context.
This is a key point in the debate on qadar (divine decree).
4
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to ensure you grasped the main point.
The question is based directly on the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل لهم : المراد بذلك والله أعلم أنه لم يكلف أحداً من الزوجات إلا ما وجد وتمكن منه دون ما لا تناله يده . ولم يرد به إثبات الاستطاعة قبل الفعل .
They were told: The intended meaning of that, and God knows best, is that He did not oblige anyone of the wives except what is found and possible for him, not what his hand cannot reach. And He did not intend by it the affirmation of capability before the action.

Commentary
This passage is an answer to an objection about the meaning of a verse concerning wives' maintenance (nafaqat). The author clarifies that the verse does not affirm the existence of capability (istita'a) prior to the action. Rather, it means that God only obligates what is present and attainable, not what is beyond one's reach. This refutes the view that capability must pre-exist the obligation.

Understanding the Ransom Verse: Two Interpretations and Abrogation
Objection and answer about the fasting ransom verse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how scholars interpret the verse about ransom for fasting, including the role of abrogation and variant readings.

Core Idea
The verse's meaning hinges on 'ability'—either to fast or to feed.

In this passage, the author addresses a potential objection regarding the verse (2:184) about those who are 'able' to fast paying a ransom. He offers two interpretations and notes that the ruling is abrogated. This shows how early scholars reconciled the verse with other texts.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice that the objection is about the meaning of a specific verse, and the author responds directly.
2
Examine the two interpretations
The author gives two possible meanings for 'able to bear it.' The first involves those who can fast but choose not to, and the verse's ruling is abrogated. The second involves those who cannot fast but can feed others.
Consider how each interpretation affects the obligation.
3
Consider the variant reading
The author mentions a variant reading 'yutawwaqunahu' meaning 'made to bear it,' which implies command without personal capacity. This reading avoids the issue of ability altogether.
Think about why the author brings up this reading.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : فما معنى قوله عزّ وجلّ : وعلى الذين يطيقونه فدية ؟ . قيل لهم : معنى ذلك على الذين يطيقون الصيام إن أرادوه وتكلّفوه وعدلوا عن الإفطار . والآية منسوخ حكمها على هذا التأويل . ويمكن أن يكون المراد وعلى الذين يطيقون الإطعام ولا يقدرون على الصيام فدية، إذا أفطروا . وقد قرىء وعلى الذين يطوقونه فدية يعني يؤمرون به ويُكلَّفونه، ولم يعرض على هذه القراءة لذكر القدرة والطاقة .
If they say: What is the meaning of His statement, Mighty and Majestic: 'And upon those who are able to bear it, a ransom'? They were told: The meaning of that is upon those who are able to fast if they intend it and undertake it and refrain from breaking the fast. And the verse's ruling is abrogated according to this interpretation. And it is possible that the meaning is: And upon those who are able to feed but cannot fast, a ransom, if they break the fast. And it has been recited: 'And upon those who are made to bear it' meaning they are commanded and obligated to do it, and this reading does not necessitate implying capability and capacity.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer regarding the verse from Surah al-Baqarah about the ransom for those who are able to fast. The author provides two interpretations: first, that it refers to those who are able to fast but choose not to, and the verse's ruling is abrogated; second, that it refers to those who are able to feed but cannot fast, so they must pay a ransom if they break the fast. The alternative reading 'yutawwaqunahu' indicates being made to bear it, which implies command without reference to personal capacity. This shows the author's exegetical method and handling of abrogation.

Misconception: One might think the verse implies that capability is a condition for obligation, but the author shows it can also mean being able to bear the cost.
Correction: The author explains that 'able' can mean either able to fast or able to feed, and the variant reading removes the notion of personal capacity entirely.

Checkpoint
According to one interpretation, what is the ruling of the verse 'On those who are able, a ransom'?
A) It is still in effect.
B) It is abrogated.
C) It applies only to the elderly.
D) It is a general obligation.

Key Takeaway
The verse's interpretation involves both abrogation and variant readings, demonstrating the nuanced approach of early exegesis.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The True Meaning of Capability for Pilgrimage
Objection and answer defining istita'a in pilgrimage verse and hypocrites' excuse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the istita'a required for pilgrimage consists of material provision and a mount, not merely physical ability, and how this applies to the hypocrites' excuse.

Core Idea
Capability for pilgrimage is provision and a mount, not just bodily strength.

This passage addresses an objection about the meaning of istita'a in the verse of Hajj. The author clarifies that the capability Allah requires is having provisions and transportation, as the Prophet explained. He also applies this understanding to the hypocrites who excused themselves from jihad – they were lacking material means, not bodily strength. We will see how this distinction resolves the apparent contradiction.

Guided Path
1

2

The author acknowledges physical capability in general but specifies the verse's meaning.
3
Apply to the hypocrites
See how the same interpretation applies to the verse about the hypocrites. Their inability was lack of mount and wealth, not physical strength.
4

Understand how this answers the objection – it shows that the verse refers to external means, not physical capability.

He extends the same interpretation to the verse about the hypocrites who swore they were not capable: the capability referred to there is material resources (mount and wealth), not bodily strength. This refutes those who might argue that capability is purely physical.

Refuting the 'Ifrit's Claim of Independent Power
Objection and answer about the 'ifrit's claim to bring the throne of Bilqis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the 'ifrit's statement in Surah an-Naml is interpreted to affirm human dependency on God's enabling, not independent power.

Core Idea
Creatures have no power except by God's enabling.

The verse about the 'ifrit in Surah an-Naml is sometimes used to argue that creatures can have independent power. In this passage, Imam Baqillani refutes that interpretation, showing that the 'ifrit's boastful claim is conditional on God's strengthening. This is a key lesson in understanding the Islamic doctrine of human dependency on Allah for all actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the passage: the opponent asks about the meaning of the 'ifrit's claim, and the author gives the response. Note the structure: question then answer.
The objection is based on apparent wording of the Quran.
2
Understand the author's interpretation
The author says the 'ifrit meant he is strong only by God's strengthening, not independent. He gives an analogy: when someone says 'I am able,' it implies 'if God enables me.'
3
Conclude the refutation
If the 'ifrit claimed independent power, he lied. Solomon likely rebuked him. Thus the verse does not prove creaturely independence.
This refutes the Qadariyyah who argued for independent human power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : فما معنى قوله : قال عفريت من الجن أنا آتيك قبل أن تقوم من مقامك وإني عليه لقوي أمين . قيل لهم : أراد هذا العفريت أنه قوي عليه في حال حمله، قواه الله على حسب ما جرت به عادته؛ كما يقول القائل : أنا أقدر أن آتيك وأخاطبك في آخر اليوم وآخر الشهر على تأويل إن أقدرني الله على ذلك، وعلى أنه يقدر في غالب الأحوال. فإن لم يكن العفريت ذلك، فقد كذب وافترى في دعواه بقدم قدرته والغنى عن ربه. ولعل سليمان صلى الله عليه قد أنكر عليه وأدبه . فلا حجة في دعوى العفاريت !
If they say: What is the meaning of His statement: 'A mighty one of the jinn said: I will bring it to you before you rise from your station, and I am indeed strong and trustworthy'? They were told: This 'ifrit meant that he was strong in carrying it, God strengthened him according to what His custom had proceeded with; just as someone says: 'I am able to come to you and address you at the end of the day or the end of the month' with the interpretation 'if God enables me,' and on the basis that he is capable in most circumstances. And perhaps Solomon, peace be upon him, rebuked him and disciplined him. So there is no proof in the claim of the 'ifrits!

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer about the 'ifrit's claim in Surah an-Naml. If the 'ifrit claimed independent power (precedence of his power and independence from God), then he would be lying. The author suggests that Solomon likely rebuked him. This refutes the argument that the 'ifrit's claim proves creatures have independent power.

The Capable Agent Is Not Compelled
Baqillani refutes an objection by defining compulsion and emphasizing choice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani distinguishes between a compelled agent and a capable agent, and why the lack of alternative actions does not imply compulsion.

Core Idea
Freedom is defined by choice and desire, not by the number of alternatives.

Baqillani refutes the claim that if an agent can only act at the moment and cannot abandon it, then he is compelled. He defines compulsion as coercion against one's will, while the capable agent acts with choice and desire. This distinction preserves human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The opponent claims that limited ability implies compulsion. Read the objection carefully.
The objection is introduced by 'فإن قالوا'.
2
Understand Baqillani's definition of compulsion
Baqillani defines the compelled person (mudtarr) as one who is coerced and acts whether willing or unwilling.
Note the Arabic: 'وأما المضطر إلى الشيء، فهو المكره'.
3
See the distinction: capable agent is a chooser
The capable agent chooses and desires his action, and is not deterred by hopes or fears. This negates compulsion.
Focus on 'والقادر على الفعل يؤثره ويهواه'.
4
Consider the reductio
Baqillani shows that if the objection were valid, even the opponent would have to say the agent is compelled, which is absurd.
This strengthens his argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة
فإن قالوا: فإذا قلتم إن القادر منا على الفعل لا يقدر عليه إلا حال حدوثه ولا يقدر على تركه وفعل ضدّه، لزمكم أن يكون في حكم المطبوع المضطر إلى الفعل.
قيل لهم: لا يجب ما قلتم لأنه ليس ها هنا مطبوع على كون شيء أو تولد عنه. وأما المضطر إلى الشيء، فهو المكره المحمول على الشيء الذي يوجد به شاء أم أبى. والقادر على الفعل يؤثره ويهواه ولا يستنزل عنه برغبة ولا رهبة فلم يجز أن يكون مضضراً مع كونه مؤثراً مختاراً. ولو كان الأمر على ما وصفتموه، لوجب أن يكون الفاعل مضطراً إليه في حاله، لأنه في تلك الحال غير قادر عندنا وعندكم على تركه. فبطل ما سألتم عنه ذلك.
Issue: If they say: Since you say that the capable one among us concerning an action is not able to do it except at the moment of its occurrence, and he is not able to abandon it and perform its opposite, then it follows that he is in the ruling of the compelled one forced to the action. It is said to them: What you say is not necessary, because there is no compelling nature here causing something or generated from it. As for the one compelled to a thing, he is the coerced, driven to the thing that occurs to him willing or unwilling. The capable one concerning the action chooses it, desires it, and is not deterred from it by desire or fear, so it is not permissible that he be compelled while being a chooser, a free agent. If the matter were as you described, then it would be necessary that the agent be compelled to it in his state, because in that state he is not capable of abandoning it according to us and according to you. So what you asked about is invalidated.
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection. The objector argues that if the agent is only capable at the moment of action and cannot abandon it or do its opposite, then he is forced (mudtarr) like something naturally compelled (matbu'). Baqillani's answer first denies that there is any natural compulsion (matbu') involved. He then defines the compelled person (mudtarr) as one who is coerced and acts whether willing or not. In contrast, the capable agent (qadir) chooses and desires the action, and is not turned away from it by any external incentive. This free choice (ikhtiyar) negates compulsion. Moreover, if the objector's logic were correct, then even according to his own view the agent would be compelled in that state, which is absurd. Thus, the objection fails.

Key Terms
قادر
مضطر
مطبوع
مكره
مختار
إيثار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if an agent has no alternative but to act, he is compelled.
Correction: Baqillani teaches that compulsion is defined by acting against one's will, not by having limited options. Choice and desire define freedom.

Checkpoint
How does Baqillani define the compelled person (mudtarr)?
A) One who cannot do an action
B) One who is coerced and acts whether willing or unwilling
C) One who chooses an action desired
D) One who has no ability to abandon an action

Key Takeaway
Baqillani establishes that a capable agent is not compelled because he acts with choice and desire, not because he has many alternatives.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a recent decision where you felt you had no other option. According to Baqillani, does that make you compelled? Examine the state of your desire and choice in that moment—were you genuinely undeterred by fear or hope? What does that reveal about your freedom?
?
Have you ever justified a sinful action by saying 'I had no choice'? This lesson defines compulsion as acting against your will. Are you willing to admit that you acted out of your own desire, and that the sin was your free choice? How will this change your repentance?
?
Consider an action you perform regularly out of habit, like a daily prayer. Are you doing it out of genuine desire and choice, or are you simply following routine? If your desire waned, would you still be capable? Challenge yourself to reclaim the free choice in your worship this week.

Non-Performance Is Abandonment, Not Incapacity
Baqillani clarifies that non-performance is due to abandonment, not incapacity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between voluntary abandonment and genuine incapacity in the context of divine commands.

Core Idea
Failure to act is due to abandonment, not lack of ability.

A common objection in kalam asks: if people fail to obey commands, does that mean they were incapable? Imam Baqillani responds concisely, affirming that they are indeed 'not capable'—but only because they abandoned the action, not because they lacked the power. This subtle distinction preserves human responsibility while acknowledging the reality of disobedience.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice how brief the exchange is.
The questioner's statement seems incomplete; Baqillani's answer clarifies it.
2
Identify the key terms
Look for the words 'قادر' (capable), 'عجز' (incapacity), and 'ترك' (abandonment). Note how they relate.
3
Understand the distinction
Baqillani agrees with the questioner's premise but redefines the cause. The 'incapacity' here is due to choice, not nature.
This preserves the Ash'ari view that ability exists before the act but can be nullified by abandoning it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة
فإن قال قائل: فجميع الذين لم يفعلوا ما أمروا به غير قادرين على
قيل له: أجل هم غير قادرين عليه لتركهم له، لا لعجزهم عنه.
Issue: If a questioner says: All those who did not do what they were commanded are not capable of [it]. It is said to him: Yes, they are not capable of it because of their abandoning it, not because of their incapacity for it.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This is a brief mas'ala addressing an objection about divine command and human failure. The questioner claims that those who do not perform commanded actions are 'not capable' (ghayr qadirin). Baqillani agrees that they are not capable, but specifies that this lack of capability is due to their abandoning the action (tarkuhum lahu), not because they are inherently incapable (ajz). The distinction is important: the ability was present, but they chose not to use it, resulting in their actual non-performance. This preserves the concept of human responsibility.

Key Terms

عجز
ترك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that failure to act implies original incapacity, i.e., the person never had the ability.
Correction: Baqillani clarifies that the inability is due to abandonment, not a lack of inherent capacity. The person could have done it but chose not to.

B) Because they abandoned the action

Key Takeaway
Baqillani teaches that failure to obey is a result of voluntary abandonment, not original incapacity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Two Meanings of 'Unable' in Divine Burdening
Baqillani distinguishes two meanings of 'unable' in burdening.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's distinction between permissible and impermissible meanings of 'unable' in the context of divine burdening.

Core Idea
Not all inability is equal: mere lack of ability is not true incapacity.

This lesson explores a key distinction in the doctrine of taklif ma la yutaq. Baqillani responds to a common objection by showing that the phrase 'not able' can refer to two different situations. Understanding this distinction is crucial to avoid misinterpreting Allah's commands.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: question, then Baqillani's two-part response.
The passage is concise; each part is important.
2
Identify the two meanings
Baqillani says the statement concerns two matters. Find both meanings of 'unable' and note how each is defined.
Meaning 1: lack of ability. Meaning 2: incapacity due to its opposite.
3
Understand why the second is impermissible
Why does true incapacity (ajz) make burdening impossible? Because it removes the person from both the commanded act and its opposite.
This is a key theological point.
4
Reflect on implications
Consider how this distinction preserves God's justice while affirming His power to command what is difficult.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة
فإن قال قائل: أتزعمون أن الله يكلف عباده ما لا يطيقون؟
قيل له: هذا كلام على أمرين. فإن أردت الطاقة بعدم على الفعل، فذلك جائز. وإن أردت بعدم الطاقة وجود ضدها من العجز، فلا يجوز ذلك. لأن العجز يُخرج عن الشيء وضدّه، ولا وجه لتكليف من هذا سبيله. وعدم القدرة على الشيء لا يوجب ذلك
Issue: If a questioner says: Do you claim that Allah burdens His servants with what they are not able to bear? It is said to him: This statement concerns two matters. If you mean ability with lack of [؟] concerning the action, then that is permissible. If you mean by lack of ability the existence of its opposite, which is incapacity, then that is not permissible. Because incapacity removes one from the thing and its opposite, and there is no aspect to burdening one in this condition. And the lack of ability for a thing does not necessitate that.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage is a distinction concerning the doctrine of 'taklif ma la yutaq' (burdening with what cannot be borne). The questioner asks if Baqillani holds that God imposes what is unbearable. Baqillani splits the answer into two possible meanings. First, if 'not able' (la yutaq) means simply a lack of ability to perform the action (like a command that one could do but doesn't), then this is permissible (ja'iz). Second, if it means a lack of ability due to the existence of its opposite, which is incapacity (ajz), then this is not permissible because incapacity removes both the thing and its opposite, making the person incapable of both doing and refraining, which is a true impossibility. The last sentence clarifies that mere lack of ability (like not having the power to do something) does not necessarily entail such an extreme incapacity.

Key Terms
تكليف
طاقة
عجز
قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'لا يطيقون' always refers to absolute impossibility, making any command of what is difficult unjust.
Correction: Baqillani shows that 'unable' can mean mere lack of ability, which does not make burdening impermissible. Only true incapacity (ajz) that negates both act and opposite is impermissible.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, which type of 'inability' makes taklif (burdening) impermissible?
A) Lack of ability to do the action
B) The existence of incapacity (ajz) as the opposite of ability
C) Any inability whatsoever
D) Inability due to lack of motivation

Key Takeaway
Baqillani shows that only incapacity due to its opposite makes burdening impermissible, while mere lack of ability does not.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself avoiding a difficult act of worship, do you ever cloak your reluctance in the excuse 'I just can't'? How does Baqillani's distinction between mere lack and true incapacity expose the difference between a real limitation and a comfortable lie you tell yourself? Commit to one specific hard command you have been avoiding, and ask: is it truly impossible, or do I simply not want to try?
?
Think of a struggle you face in obeying Allah. Are you quick to label it as 'too hard' or 'beyond me'? This lesson forces you to confront: are you using 'inability' as a shield against accountability? What would it take for you to honestly examine whether your 'cannot' is actually 'will not'? Resolve today to stop hiding behind false inability.
?
Baqillani teaches that true incapacity removes both the act and its opposite. Look at your life: are there commands you have abandoned entirely, claiming you are incapable of even approaching them? Have you considered that the opposite—disobedience—is also within your reach, so you are not truly incapacitated? Where are you deceiving yourself by calling your negligence 'impossible'? Write down one area and commit to taking a small step tonight.

Refutation of Compulsion from God's Action
Baqillani refutes the claim that God's action necessitates human compulsion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani refutes the argument that God's action compels the human agent by distinguishing between being influenced and being compelled.

Core Idea
Being influenced by desire is not the same as being compelled to act.

Imam Baqillani addresses a key objection in the debate on human agency and divine action. Opponents claimed that if God is the true agent, then humans are compelled. Baqillani counters by showing that the agent's motivation (desire/fear) indicates freedom, not compulsion. Let's examine his precise argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the concise logical structure.
The passage is a single continuous argument.
2
Identify the objection being refuted
The passage starts by refuting an implied objection. Look for the conditional 'if the matter were as you described'. This signals the opponent's claim.
The objector is not named, but the argument is clear.
3
Trace the conditional logic
Baqillani uses a reductio: assume the objector's claim is true, then the agent would be compelled and unable to leave the action. This contradicts the agent's description as free and effective.
The conclusion is that the objection is invalidated.
4
Reflect on the key distinction
Note that being influenced by desire or fear (رغبة ورهبة) is presented as a sign of free choice, not compulsion. This is the core refutation.
This distinction is crucial in Islamic theology of action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يستنزل عنه برغبة ولا رهبة فلم يجز أن يكون مضطراً مع كونه مؤثراً مختاراً. ولو كان الأمر على ما وصفتموه، لوجب أن يكون الفاعل مضطراً إليه في حاله، لأنه في تلك الحال غير قادر عندنا وعندكم للشيء على تركه. فبطل ما سألتم عنه ذلك.
He is not swayed from Him by desire or fear, so it is not permissible that he be compelled while being effective and free. And if the matter were as you described, then the doer would have to be compelled to it in his state, because in that state he is not able, according to us and you, to leave it. So what you asked about is invalidated.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage refutes an objection that God's action compels the human agent. The author argues that the agent is described as being moved by desire or fear, not as being compelled. If the objector's description were true, the agent would be forced and unable to refrain, which contradicts the agent's free and effective nature. The argument is a reductio: assume the premise, then the conclusion is absurd, so the premise is false.

Key Terms
مضطر
مؤثر
مختار
غير قادر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Being influenced by desire or fear means the agent is compelled.
Correction: Baqillani distinguishes between being influenced (which implies choice) and being compelled (which removes choice).

Checkpoint
What does the passage conclude about the agent if he is 'مضطر' (compelled)?
A) He would be free and effective.
B) He would be unable to refrain from the action.
C) He would be both compelled and free.
D) He would be completely independent.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani refutes the compulsion argument by showing that if the agent were compelled, he would be unable to refrain, but the agent's motivation proves he is free.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If you truly believe that God's actions do not compel you, then your gratitude for blessings and your remorse for sins are genuine. Which area of your life are you currently blaming on God's decree instead of your own free will? Resolve to take full accountability starting today.

Inability as a Consequence of Abandonment
Baqillani explains inability after abandonment results from neglect, not incapacity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that being 'unable' to perform a commanded act can be a result of one's own abandonment rather than inherent incapacity.

Core Idea
Inability can be a consequence of neglect, not a lack of original ability.

Baqillani presents a brief but critical clarification in the debate about human ability and divine command. A potential objector claims that those who fail to obey are simply unable. The refutation sharpens the distinction between original capacity and actual performance — affirming that the inability is self-inflicted through abandonment.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the dialectical exchange. Notice the structure: a hypothetical objection followed by a concise refutation.
The objector's phrasing assumes inherent inability; the response changes the basis.
2
Identify the key terms
Focus on the terms 'غير قادرين' (unable), 'لتركهم' (because of their abandonment), and 'عجز' (incapacity). These frame the theological point.
The distinction between 'عجز' (incapacity) and 'عدم القدرة' (lack of ability due to abandonment) is subtle but crucial.
3
Understand the theological nuance
Reflect on why this distinction matters: it preserves human responsibility while affirming divine justice. The ability was present but not exercised, leading to inability as a consequence.
This aligns with the Ash'ari position that human acts are acquired and that ability is not simultaneous with the act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال قائل : فجميع الذين لم يفعلوا ما أمروا به غير قادرين على قيل له أجل هم غير قادرين عليه لتركهم له، لا لعجزهم عنه.
If someone says: 'Then all those who did not do what they were commanded are unable to do it.' It is said to him: 'Yes, they are unable to do it because of their abandonment of it, not because of their incapacity for it.'
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This is a brief objection and answer. The objector claims that those who fail to obey are 'unable' (non posse). The answer affirms that they are indeed unable, but the inability is a consequence of their own abandonment, not an inherent incapacity. This preserves the idea that they had the ability originally but lost it through neglect.

Key Terms
غير قادرين
لتركهم
عجز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why are those who did not perform what they were commanded unable to perform it?
A) Because they are inherently incapable.
B) Because they abandoned it.
C) Because God did not give them ability.
D) Because they chose not to.

Key Takeaway
Those who fail to obey are unable solely because they abandoned the act, not because of an inherent lack of capacity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?

Two Meanings of 'Cannot Bear' in Divine Obligation
Baqillani distinguishes two meanings of 'not being able' regarding divine obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between two senses of 'not being able' in the context of God's command, and why only one sense can apply to divine obligation.

Core Idea
Not being able is not the same as being unable.

Imam Baqillani addresses a critical question: Does God command what cannot be done? The answer depends on what 'cannot' means. This passage clarifies two distinct senses, protecting divine justice from misunderstanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the question posed
Begin by reading the question: 'Do you claim that God burdens His servants with what they cannot bear?' This is a common objection against divine justice.
2
Identify the two meanings
The response says 'this statement has two aspects'. Find the two definitions of 'ability' (طاقة) that Baqillani gives.
The first meaning relates to not performing the act; the second involves actual incapacity.
3
Understand why the second is impermissible
Reflect on why the second meaning (incapacity, عجز) cannot apply to divine command. The passage explains that incapacity removes one from the thing and its opposite, making obligation futile.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this distinction resolves the original objection. It shows that God only commands what is within one's capacity, even if one does not perform it.
This guards against the misconception that God imposes impossible burdens.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال قائل : أتزعمون أن الله يكلف عباده ما لا يطيقون؟ قيل له : هذا كلام على أمرين. فإن أردت الطاقة بعدم على الفعل، فذلك جائز. وإن أردت بعدم الطاقة وجود ضدها من العجز، فلا يجوز ذلك. لأن العجز يُخرج عن الشيء وضدّه، ولا وجه لتكليف من هذا سبيله. وعدم القدرة على الشيء لا يوجب ذلك.
If someone says: 'Do you claim that God burdens His servants with what they cannot bear?' It is said to him: 'This statement has two aspects. If you mean ability by absence of the act, then that is permissible. If you mean ability by the presence of its opposite, incapacity, then that is not permissible. Because incapacity removes one from the thing and its opposite, and there is no sense in burdening one who is in this state. And inability regarding a thing does not necessitate that.'
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage defines two senses of 'لا يطيقون' (cannot bear). The first sense is simply not having the ability due to not performing the act (i.e., not having the power to do it because one didn't do it). The second sense is having an actual incapacity (عجز) that prevents both the act and its opposite. The latter cannot be a burden because the person is excused. The distinction guards against misunderstanding divine justice.

Key Terms
طاقة
عجز
تكليف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God cannot command what is not done because it would be unjust.
Correction: The passage shows that 'not doing' (lack of performance) is not the same as 'inability' (incapacity); God can command what is not done as long as the person has the capacity to do it.

Checkpoint
What is the first meaning of 'طاقة' according to the passage?
A) Power to do the act.
B) Absence of the act itself.
C) Presence of incapacity.
D) Both B and C.

Key Takeaway
This passage shows that the objection 'God commands what cannot be done' is ambiguous: it is only an objection if 'cannot' means inherent incapacity, but if it means simply not doing it, it is not an objection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you fail to obey a command of Allah, do you tend to think it was too difficult for you, or do you honestly admit that you simply chose not to do it? How does Baqillani's distinction force you to reconsider your excuses?
?

?
This lesson shows that God only commands what is within our capacity. Is there a command you have been avoiding because you believe it is beyond you? What would it mean to trust that Allah knows you can do it, and to take the first step today?
