Human Burdening Beyond Capacity
Objection and beginning of response on human burdening.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Baqillani argues that burdening beyond capacity is not exclusive to God; humans can also do so analogously, as shown by everyday examples.

Core Idea
Humans can also impose burdens beyond capacity, as seen in everyday commands.

This passage addresses a potential objection: is it only God who can impose burdens beyond capacity? Baqillani responds by affirming that humans do so routinely, giving the example of ordering a sitting person to stand. This sets the stage for a broader discussion of theodicy and human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Notice the question-and-answer format.
The passage is incomplete, but the core idea is clear.
2
Identify the objection
The objector asks: 'Is such burdening good from us or permissible for us?' This refers to burdening someone beyond their capacity.
The objector doubts whether humans can do this.
3
Understand the affirmative answer
Baqillani answers 'Yes' and provides an example: we often burden a sitting person with standing and acting while seated. This shows humans can impose such burdens.
The example illustrates that the command is possible even if the person is currently sitting.
4
Reflect on the broader implication
If humans can do this, then God's similar actions are not unjust. This analogy will be developed further in the full argument.
This is a key move in theodicy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال قائل : وهل يحسن مثل هذا التكليف منا أو يسوغ لنا؟ قيل له : أجل، لأنَّا قد نكلف القاعد القيام والتصرف في حال قعوده،
If someone says: 'Is such burdening good from us or permissible for us?' It is said to him: 'Yes, because we often burden the sitting one with standing and acting while he is sitting,...'
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This is a question about whether humans can permissibly impose a burden beyond capacity. The answer begins by affirming yes, giving an example of ordering a sitting person to stand while he is sitting. The argument likely continues that the person is able to do it if he chooses. The passage is incomplete.

Correction: Baqillani argues that humans commonly impose such burdens, e.g., ordering a seated person to stand. Thus the action is not exclusive to God.

Checkpoint

Baqillani defends the permissibility of humans imposing burdens beyond capacity by analogy with ordinary commands like ordering a seated person to stand.

The Dilemma of Interpreting Verses on Creation
Baqillani responds to an objection about interpreting a Quranic verse on creators.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses a logical parallel to defend the comprehensive negation of other creators in the Quran.

Core Idea
If you restrict one verse, you must consistently restrict the other—leaving religion or admitting error.

Baqillani engages an opponent who tries to limit the Quranic negation of other creators to only those who provide sustenance. He responds by drawing a sharp parallel with another verse about god and night, then forces a dilemma: consistency leads to disbelief, inconsistency to rejection of their interpretation. This dialectical move is a classic kalam technique.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. First, understand the opponent's position: they claim the verse only negates a creator who provides. Then see how Baqillani counters with an analogy.
The opponent's verse likely referred to ‘Who provides for you from the heaven and the earth?’ (Q 35:3).
2
Analyze the parallel argument
Notice that Baqillani uses another verse about bringing the night (e.g., Q 28:71-72). He says the same logic applies: just as the first verse negates a provider, the second negates a night-bringer. The opponent cannot restrict one without restricting the other.
This forces the opponent to choose between consistency and their original interpretation.
3
Understand the dilemma
The author presents the consequences: if the opponent accepts the parallel (i.e., both verses only negate specific acts), then they have effectively denied that Allah alone creates all things—abandoning the religion. If they reject the parallel, their interpretation of the first verse is arbitrary and invalid.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : إنما نفى خالقاً غيره يرزق من السماء والأرض . قيل لهم : وكذلك إنما نفى إلهاً غيره يأتي بليل تسكنون فيه . فإن مروا على هذا فارقوا الدين ؛ وإن أبوا أبينا تأويلهم.
If they say: He only negated a creator other than Him who provides from the heaven and the earth. It is said to them: Likewise He only negated a god other than Him who brings night in which you rest. If they pass over this, they leave the religion; and if they refuse, we reject their interpretation.
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange between an opponent and the author. The opponent claims that the Quranic verse (presumably 'Say: Who provides for you from the heaven and the earth?') only negates a creator who provides sustenance, not all creators. The author counters by drawing a parallel: similarly, another verse about bringing the night negates a god who brings night, and the same logic applies. The author then presents a logical dilemma: if the opponents accept this parallel argument, they abandon the religion (because they would be denying that God is the sole creator of all things); if they reject it, their own interpretation of the first verse is invalid because it fails by analogy.

Key Terms
خالق (creator)
إله (god)
تأويل (interpretation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Quranic negation of other creators only applies to acts of providing sustenance, not to all acts of creation.
Correction: Baqillani shows that the same reasoning would apply to any specific act (e.g., bringing night), so if one restricts the scope, one must restrict all such verses consistently, which leads to absurdity. The correct understanding is that these verses negate any other creator or god completely.

Checkpoint
What does the author argue would happen if the opponents accept the parallel interpretation?
A) They would be correct.
B) They would leave the religion.
C) They would strengthen their argument.
D) They would have to find another verse.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani uses a parallel verse and a dilemma to show that restricting the negation of other creators leads to disbelief or logical inconsistency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself limiting the meaning of Allah's attributes or actions to fit a comfortable understanding? What would it cost you to accept the full, uncompromised scope of His creatorship in every detail of your life? Will you today repent from any mental reservation and embrace tawheed without exception?
?
Imagine you are the opponent in Baqillani's dilemma: would you rather leave the religion by allowing other creators for some acts, or reject your own interpretation and submit fully? How does your heart lean, and what does that reveal about your current state of iman?
?
The passage forces a choice between consistency and comfort. In what area of your faith are you tempted to make Allah 'Small' by restricting His power to only certain spheres? What step will you take this week to extend His lordship to that very area?

The First Quranic Proof: None Create Besides Allah
Baqillani cites Quran 16:20 and comments on its implication for angel worship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Surah An-Nahl 16:20 proves that nothing worshipped besides Allah can create, and why even angels are not creators.

Core Idea
Anything worshipped besides Allah is itself created, so it cannot create.

Imam al-Baqillani begins his scriptural proofs with a clear verse from the Quran that directly negates creation from any being other than Allah. He points out that even angels, whom some have worshipped, are included in this negation. This verse establishes a fundamental principle: only Allah is the Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and translation
Read the Arabic verse from Surah An-Nahl 16:20 and its English translation. Notice the clear negation of creative power for those invoked besides Allah.
2
Identify the negation
The verse says 'la yakhluqoona shay'an' (they create nothing). This is a general negation covering all beings worshipped besides Allah.
3
Understand the angel example
Baqillani highlights that angels, though honored, are included—they are created and cannot create. This refutes any idea that angel worship is justified.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويدل على ذلك أيضاً قوله تعالى : والذين يدعون من دون الله لا يخلقون شيئاً وهم يخلقون . وقد عبدت الإنس الملائكة، وقد نفى الله تعالى أن يكونوا خالقين لشيء على وجه . ويدل على ذلك أيضاً قوله تعالى : أم جعلوا الله شركاء خلقوا كخلقه فتشابه الخلق عليهم . فحكم تعالى بشرك من ادعى أنه خلقه . فلو كان العباد يخلقون كلامهم وحركاتهم وسكونهم وإرادتهم وعلومهم ، وهذه الأجناس أجمع كخلقه ومن جنس ما يوجده ، لكانوا قد خلقوا كخلقه وصنعوا كصنعه، ولتشابه على الخلق خلقه وخلقهم تعالى عن ذلك !
And this is also indicated by His statement, the Most High: 'And those they invoke besides Allah create nothing, and they themselves are created' (16:20). And humans have worshipped the angels, and Allah the Most High has negated that they are creators of anything at all. And this is also indicated by His statement, the Most High: 'Or have they assigned to Allah partners who created like His creation, so that the creation seemed alike to them?' (13:16). So the Most High judged as polytheism the claim of anyone that it is His creation. So if servants created their speech, movements, stillness, will, and knowledge, and all these kinds together are like His creation and of the same genus as what He brings into existence, then they would have created like His creation and made like His making, and His creation and their creation would have seemed alike to the creation—exalted is He above that!
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage provides two scriptural proofs for the doctrine that no one besides Allah creates anything. The first verse (16:20) states that those worshipped besides Allah create nothing and are themselves created. The author notes that even the angels, whom some have worshipped, are negated as creators. The second verse (13:16) declares that anyone who claims partners for Allah who create like His creation is guilty of polytheism (shirk). The author then applies this: if human beings were to create their own actions—speech, movements, will, etc.—that would be comparable to Allah's creation, and thus they would be associating partners with Him. The conclusion is that such a notion is impossible, and Allah is exalted above having any partners in creation.

Key Terms
خالق (creator)
يخلقون (they create)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think angels or other beings can create.
Correction: The verse clearly states that those invoked besides Allah create nothing and are themselves created.

Checkpoint
According to Quran 16:20, those invoked besides Allah...
A) create nothing
B) create only good things
C) create with permission
D) create but are themselves eternal

Key Takeaway
Quran 16:20 establishes that no being other than Allah has any creative power; even angels are created and not creators.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a beautiful sunset, do you ever attribute its creation to nature or chance? But the verse says everything besides Allah is created and creates nothing. Tomorrow, when you witness any natural phenomenon, consciously say, 'Allah created this.' Will you make that your habit?
?
Who or what do you rely on for your daily provision—your job, your skills, your spouse? The verse proves they cannot create anything, including your sustenance. Today, identify one thing you have credited to a created cause and return that credit to Allah. Are you willing to do that immediately after this lesson?
?
When you feel anxious, do you instinctively seek relief from people, distractions, or your own efforts? But none of these can 'create' peace in your heart. This verse demands you turn only to the Creator. Write down one specific anxiety you are facing and commit to invoking Allah alone for it tonight. What will you say?

The Second Quranic Proof: Partners in Creation Would Be Shirk
Baqillani cites Quran 13:16 and applies it to human actions to refute human creativity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Surah Ar-Ra'd 13:16 refutes the idea that humans create their own actions, as it would imply shirk.

Core Idea
If humans created their actions, it would resemble Allah's creation, leading to polytheism.

Imam al-Baqillani now cites a second verse that warns against assigning partners to Allah who create like His creation. He then applies this principle to human actions: if people create their own speech, movements, and will, that would make them like Allah's creation, which is impossible. This verse deepens the argument that only Allah creates.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and its ruling
Read the Arabic verse from Surah Ar-Ra'd 13:16 and its translation. Notice that Allah judges as shirk anyone who claims partners creating like His creation.
2
Apply to human actions
Baqillani applies the verse to human actions: if humans created their own actions, those actions would be similar to Allah's creation, leading to confusion. This cannot be true.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends with 'ta'ala 'an dhalik' (exalted is He above that), showing the impossibility of anyone sharing in creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويدل على ذلك أيضاً قوله تعالى : والذين يدعون من دون الله لا يخلقون شيئاً وهم يخلقون . وقد عبدت الإنس الملائكة، وقد نفى الله تعالى أن يكونوا خالقين لشيء على وجه . ويدل على ذلك أيضاً قوله تعالى : أم جعلوا الله شركاء خلقوا كخلقه فتشابه الخلق عليهم . فحكم تعالى بشرك من ادعى أنه خلقه . فلو كان العباد يخلقون كلامهم وحركاتهم وسكونهم وإرادتهم وعلومهم ، وهذه الأجناس أجمع كخلقه ومن جنس ما يوجده ، لكانوا قد خلقوا كخلقه وصنعوا كصنعه، ولتشابه على الخلق خلقه وخلقهم تعالى عن ذلك !
And this is also indicated by His statement, the Most High: 'And those they invoke besides Allah create nothing, and they themselves are created' (16:20). And humans have worshipped the angels, and Allah the Most High has negated that they are creators of anything at all. And this is also indicated by His statement, the Most High: 'Or have they assigned to Allah partners who created like His creation, so that the creation seemed alike to them?' (13:16). So the Most High judged as polytheism the claim of anyone that it is His creation. So if servants created their speech, movements, stillness, will, and knowledge, and all these kinds together are like His creation and of the same genus as what He brings into existence, then they would have created like His creation and made like His making, and His creation and their creation would have seemed alike to the creation—exalted is He above that!
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage provides two scriptural proofs for the doctrine that no one besides Allah creates anything. The first verse (16:20) states that those worshipped besides Allah create nothing and are themselves created. The author notes that even the angels, whom some have worshipped, are negated as creators. The second verse (13:16) declares that anyone who claims partners for Allah who create like His creation is guilty of polytheism (shirk). The author then applies this: if human beings were to create their own actions—speech, movements, will, etc.—that would be comparable to Allah's creation, and thus they would be associating partners with Him. The conclusion is that such a notion is impossible, and Allah is exalted above having any partners in creation.

Key Terms
شركاء (partners)
خلق (creation)
يخلقون (they create)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think humans create their own actions.
Correction: The Quranic verse and Baqillani's argument show that if humans created their actions, it would be shirk, so it must be false.

Checkpoint
According to the second verse (Quran 13:16), what does Allah call anyone who claims that others create like His creation?
A) Truthful
B) Polytheist (mushrik)
C) Forgiven
D) Uninformed

Key Takeaway
Quran 13:16 establishes that claiming any created being creates like Allah is shirk; thus humans cannot be creators of their own actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you secretly feel that you created that action yourself, as if you had independent power? How does this verse (13:16) dismantle that pride and redirect your reliance entirely to Allah?
?
If every movement of your tongue is not your creation but Allah's, then what fear of shirk should you have when you boast about your good deeds? Will you now attribute every good word to His grace alone?
?
The verse calls any claim of creation besides Allah 'polytheism.' Is there any area of your life where you act as if you are the creator—your career, your relationships, your character? What would it mean to truly submit those to Allah as the sole Creator?

God Creates, Man Acquires: Answering the Power Sharing Objection
Baqillani refutes the objection that if God creates actions, both have power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's refutation of the objection that if God creates human actions, both God and man would have power over them, and how the Ash'ari concept of kasb resolves this.

Core Idea
God creates, man acquires—no shared power over the same act.

This lesson examines a key objection to the Ash'ari doctrine of divine creation of actions. The opponents argue that if God creates our acts, we would share power with Him, leading to absurd contradictions. Baqillani responds by clarifying the role of human power before the act's existence and the exclusive divine creative act. Read the passage to see the precise logical argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Identify the opponents' claim and the response.
Notice the logical flow: objection → consequence → refutation.
2
Understand the opponents' argument
The opponents claim that if God creates actions, both have power, leading to the absurdity that one thing could be both done and left undone.
3
Grasp Baqillani's key distinction
Baqillani says human power only applies to an action before it exists. Once it exists, man cannot leave it. God is the exclusive creator (mujid), so only He can bring it into existence.
This avoids the absurdity of shared power over the same act.
4
Learn the concept of kasb
The passage uses 'yaksibu' (acquires) for man's role. This is the Ash'ari doctrine of acquisition: God creates, man acquires responsibility.
This preserves both divine sovereignty and human accountability.
5
Reflect on the resolution
Consider how this argument defends the Ash'ari position against the objection of shared power.
The passage is a concise example of kalam reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذكر شبه لهم ونقضها قد قالوا : لو كانت أفعالنا خلقاً الله ، لكانت مقدورة لنا وله . ولو كان ذلك كذلك، لجاز أن نفعلها ويتركها هو أو نتركها ويفعلها، فيكون الشيء الواحد مفعولاً متروكاً. قيل لهم : هذا باطل، لأن الإنسان لا يقدر على الفعل إلا في حال يجوز أن يتركه في حال قد وجد فيها ولا يجوز أيضاً أن يتركه وجوده، فلا الله في تلك الحال، لأنه هو الموجد لعينه دون العبد الذي يكسبه، فلو تركه لم يكن موجوداً.
Mention of their doubts and their refutation. They said: If our actions were the creation of God, they would be within our power and His power. If that were the case, it would be possible for us to do them and He to leave them, or for us to leave them and He to do them, so the same thing would be both done and left. It is said to them: This is false, because man is not able to do an action except in a state in which it is possible for him to leave it, but in a state in which it has already come to be, he cannot leave it either, because He (God) is the one who brings that action into existence without the servant who acquires (yaksibu) it. So if He left it, it would not exist.
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This is an objection-and-answer pair within the section 'Mention of their doubts and their refutation'. The opponents argue that if God creates human actions, then both God and humans have power over them, leading to absurdity: one thing could be both done and left undone. The author refutes by explaining that human power (qudra) only applies to an action before its existence; once it exists, it cannot be undone. Moreover, the actual creator (majid) is God alone; man merely 'acquires' (kash) the action. Therefore, it is impossible for the same action to be simultaneously done by man and left by God—God creates it, and man cannot create it. The argument shows the logical coherence of the Ash'ari doctrine of kasb.

Key Terms
خلق
مقدور
موجد
كسب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God creates all actions, humans have no real power and are not responsible.
Correction: The author clarifies that humans have power before the action exists and acquire it, while God is the sole creator. This maintains human responsibility without shared power.

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the claim that if God creates actions, the same action could be both done and left?
A) He agrees it is a problem.
B) He says humans have power even after the action exists.
C) He explains that human power only pertains to the action before it exists, and God creates it exclusively.
D) He argues that actions are not really created.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani shows that since God alone creates actions and human power precedes existence, the absurdity of shared power over the same act is avoided, preserving the Ash'ari doctrine of divine sovereignty and human acquisition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you truly feel that you are the one who chose it, yet also that only God could have brought it into existence? How does this tension sit in your heart, and what does it demand of you in terms of repentance?
?
If God alone creates every action, including your prayers and your sins, what becomes of your personal responsibility? Are you willing to stand before Him and say 'I did it' while acknowledging He created the deed? How does that change your attitude towards your own good deeds and failures?
?
This lesson shows that your power over an action exists only before it happens. Are you using that brief moment of choice to turn to God and ask for His help, or do you pretend your power is absolute and independent? What will you change tomorrow morning to live this truth?

God Creates Both Intention and Action
Baqillani refutes the objection that actions being from our intentions proves they are our creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani refutes the argument that actions following our intentions prove we are their creators, by showing that God could be the creator of both the intention and the action.

Core Idea
Intentions do not make us creators; God creates both our intentions and our actions.

In this passage, Baqillani addresses a second objection from those who claim that because our actions align with our intentions and we can refrain at will, we must be the creators of our actions. He responds with a simple but powerful counter: what if God creates both the intention and the action? This lesson unpacks that exchange.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the objection
Read the objection carefully: actions occur by our intentions and will, and we can refrain. Ask: why does this seem to prove human creation?
The objection is intuitive but the answer reveals a deeper possibility.
2
Understand the answer
Baqillani suggests an alternative: God creates the action and also the intention, and when we refrain, God simply does not create the action. Understand how this preserves God's exclusive creative power.
3
Evaluate the force of the answer
Notice that the objection assumes a direct causal link between intention and action, but Baqillani points out that correlation does not necessitate creation. The answer shows that the opponents have no ground to reject this.
4
Connect to broader kalām principle
This reflects the Ashʿarī principle that only God creates, and human actions are acquired (kasb). Reflect on how this answer fits into the larger debate on free will and divine omnipotence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : الدليل على أن أفعالنا خلق لنا كونها واقعة بقصودنا وإرادتنا وامتناعنا منها إذا شئنا . قيل لهم : ما أنكرتم أن يكون الله هو الخالق لها والخالق لقصودكم إليها، وهو التارك لخلقها في حال انصرافكم عنها وإعراضكم عن القصد إلى اكتسابها؟ فلا يجدون في ذلك متعلقاً.
If they say: The proof that our actions are our creation is that they occur according to our intentions and will, and our refraining from them when we wish. It is said to them: What prevents you from saying that God is the creator of them and the creator of your intentions toward them, and He is the one who refrains from creating them when you turn away from them and withdraw from intending to acquire them? So they will find no basis in that.
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This is the second objection-answer pair. The opponents argue that since actions follow our intentions and we can refrain, this proves we create them. The author responds by suggesting an alternative: God creates both the action and the intention (the decision to act). When we refrain, God simply does not create the action at that moment. Thus the correlation between intention and action does not necessitate that humans create the action—God could be the direct creator of both the intention and the act, while human choice is the occasion for God's creative action. The opponents, the author notes, have no ground to reject this explanation.

Key Terms
قصد (intention)
إرادة (will)
انصراف (turning away)
اكتساب (acquisition)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If actions follow our intentions, then we must be the creators of those actions.
Correction: Baqillani shows that God could create both the intention and the action, so the correlation does not force human creation.

Checkpoint
What does the author suggest as an alternative explanation for the connection between intention and action?
A) Humans create both intention and action.
B) God creates only the action, not the intention.
C) God creates both the intention and the action, and refrains from creating the action when we turn away.
D) Intentions are irrelevant to actions.

Key Takeaway
The correlation between intention and action does not prove human creation; God could be the creator of both, preserving His exclusive creative power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you intend to do a good deed, do you truly recognize that even that intention is a creation of God, placed in your heart? How does that affect your reliance on yourself versus reliance on Him? Commit to thanking Him for every good intention He gives you.
?
If you have previously thought that your ability to refrain from sin is due to your own strength, how will you now view your refraining after learning that it is God who refrains from creating the sin at that moment? Does this change how you thank Him? Take a moment to express gratitude for each instance of refraining.
?
Recall a time you felt proud of a good action you performed. In light of this lesson, what is the reality of your contribution? How can you ensure your intentions remain pure, knowing that even your intention is created by God? Resolve to check your motives and attribute all good to Him.

Why Divine Commands Don't Contradict Divine Creation
Baqillani refutes the objection that divine commands are incompatible with creation of acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani refutes the objection that God's commands are meaningless if He creates all actions, by offering alternative models for command and acquisition.

Core Idea
God commands acquisition, not creation.

Baqillani addresses a key objection from those who believe humans create their own actions: how can God command or forbid if He creates everything? The objection seems strong, but Baqillani shows it rests on an unwarranted assumption. He offers two plausible alternatives that preserve both divine sovereignty and moral accountability.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Start by reading the opponent's argument. They claim that if God creates all actions, then His commands and prohibitions would be invalid. Why would that be? Think about it.
The key is that commanding oneself to do something is futile.
2
See Baqillani's initial response
Notice Baqillani's immediate dismissal: 'Why do you say that?' This is a crucial move—he demands justification for the link between creation and command.
The burden of proof is on the opponent.
3
Explore the first alternative
Baqillani suggests that creation is impossible for the servant; God only commands acquisition (kash) of the actions He creates. Understand the distinction: God creates the act, the servant acquires it. The command applies to acquisition, not creation.
4
Explore the second alternative
The second possibility: actions are mere signs (alam) for reward or punishment, with no direct command attached. This further undermines the objection.
5
Reflect on the lesson
Consider why these alternatives are sufficient to refute the objection. The opponent must show that the only possible model is the one they assume; Baqillani shows otherwise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : الدليل على أنه لا يجوز أن تكون أفعالنا خلقاً لله تعالى أنه لو كان هو الخالق لها، لم يصح أمره بها ونهيه عن بعضها وإثابته على الحسن الجميل منها وعقابه على القبيح من جملتها . قيل لهم : لم قلتم ذلك ؟ فلا يجدون في ذلك وجهاً. ثم يقال لهم : ما أنكرتم أن لا يكون الله تعالى آمراً لأحد من خلقه بخلق شيء من الأفعال ولا ناهياً له عن ذلك ولا مثيباً لأحد على أن خلق شيئاً ولا معاقباً له على ذلك، لأن الخلق مستحيل من العبد ؛ وأن يكون إنما أمر باكتساب ما خلقه ونهى عن ذلك وأثاب وعاقب وذمّ ومدح ووعد وتوعد على أن اكتسب العبد ما نهى عنه وأمر به فقط ؟ بل ما أنكرتم أن يكون إنما جعل هذه الأفعال علماً على إثابة من أحبّ إثابته وعقاب من أحب عقابه فقط ؟
If they say: The proof that our actions cannot be a creation of Allah the Most High is that if He were their creator, His command regarding them and His prohibition of some of them and His reward for the good and beautiful among them and His punishment for the evil among them would not be valid. It is said to them: Why do you say that? So they will find no basis in that. Then it is said to them: What prevents you from saying that Allah the Most High is not a commander to any of His creation to create any action, nor a prohibitor to him from that, nor a rewarder to anyone for creating something, nor a punisher to him for that, because creation is impossible from the servant; and that He only commanded the acquisition (kash) of what He created and prohibited that, and rewarded and punished and blamed and praised and promised and threatened for that the servant acquired what He prohibited and commanded, only? Or what prevents you from saying that He only made these actions as a sign (alam) for rewarding whom He wills to reward and punishing whom He wills to punish, only?
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This is the third objection-answer pair. The opponents argue that if God creates all actions, divine commands and prohibitions would be meaningless (commanding oneself or contradicting). The author first dismisses this as baseless: 'Why do you say that?' Then he offers two alternative explanations: (1) God does not command anyone to create actions (because creation is impossible for man), but rather He commands the 'acquisition' (kash) of the actions He creates. So the command applies to the human act of acquisition, not to the creation. Reward and punishment are for the acquisition, not for the creation. (2) God may simply make these actions as signs (alam) to indicate whom He will reward or punish, without any direct command or prohibition attached to the action itself. This shows that the opponents' inference is not necessary; other interpretations are possible.

Key Terms
خلق (creation)
اكتساب (acquisition)
أمر (command)
نهي (prohibition)
علم (sign)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God creates all actions, then divine commands and prohibitions are logically impossible.
Correction: Baqillani shows that God commands the acquisition (kash) of the actions He creates, not the creation itself, so commands remain meaningful.

Checkpoint
According to one of the author's alternative explanations, to what does God's command apply?
A) To the creation of actions.
B) To the acquisition (kash) of the actions He creates.
C) To both creation and acquisition.
D) To nothing—commands are meaningless.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani refutes the objection by showing that God's commands apply to human acquisition, not to the divine creation of actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you instinctively attribute it to your own choice or to Allah's creation? How does this lesson's distinction between creation and acquisition challenge you to take full ownership of your actions while still relying on Allah? This week, after every action, pause and say: 'I acquired this, but Allah created it.'
?
If you truly believed that your obedience and disobedience are signs (alam) of Allah's will for you, would that change how you strive in worship? Would you work harder knowing that your efforts are signs of His decree, or would you become passive? Ask yourself: am I using divine decree as an excuse for laziness?
?
The opponents' objection sounds logical—if God creates actions, why command? But the author dismisses it as baseless. Are there areas in your faith where you assume a contradiction between God's attributes? How will you from now on trust that He has wise explanations even when you don't see them?

Refuting the Objection that Intentions Prove Human Creation
Objection and refutation on intention as proof of human creation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Baqillani refutes the argument that human intentions prove that actions are created by humans.

Core Idea
Intention does not prove independent creation; God creates both action and intention.

In this passage, a common objection against Ash'ari theology is presented and refuted. The objector claims that since our actions align with our intentions and we can refrain from them, they must be our own creation. Imam al-Baqillani turns the argument around, showing that even the intention is created by God, and our ability to refrain is simply God not creating the action. This lesson unpacks this critical rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objector's claim in the Arabic and translation. Note their reasoning: actions are linked to intentions and will, and we can refrain from them when we wish.
2
Identify the underlying assumption
Notice that the objector assumes that if an action is connected to our intention, it must be our own creation. This is the premise the author will challenge.
3
Understand the author's refutation
The author responds by asking: why can't God create both the action and the intention? When we refrain, God simply does not create the action at that moment. This counters the objector's premise.
4
Reflect on the implication for human agency
Consider how this refutation preserves God's sole creative power while allowing for human responsibility through acquisition (kasb), not direct creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : الدليل على أن أفعالنا خلق لنا كونها واقعة بقصودنا وإرادتنا وامتناعنا منها إذا شئنا . قيل لهم : ما أنكرتم أن يكون الله هو الخالق لها والخالق لقصودكم إليها، وهو التارك لخلقها في حال انصرافكم عنها وإعراضكم عن القصد إلى اكتسابها؟ فلا يجدون في ذلك متعلقاً .

The objector argues that human actions must be created by humans because they are linked to our intentions and wills, and because we can deliberately refrain from them. The author responds by challenging the underlying assumption: why cannot God be the creator of both the action and the intention? When we turn away from an action, God simply does not create it at that moment. Thus, the objector's argument provides no real evidence for human creation.

Divine Creation and Moral Accountability
Objection and brief refutation regarding divine creation and moral accountability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Baqillani refutes the objection that divine creation of actions negates moral accountability by challenging its logical necessity.

Core Idea
Divine creation of actions does not automatically invalidate command and reward.

This passage presents a key objection from those who deny that God creates human actions. They argue that if God were the creator, then His commands and punishments would be meaningless. Imam al-Baqillani's reply is deceptively simple: he asks for proof. This lesson examines that objection and the critical response.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the claim that divine creation nullifies moral commands. The objector asserts that if God creates all actions, then commanding, prohibiting, rewarding, and punishing become invalid.
Notice the objector's assumption of a necessary entailment.
2
Analyze the author's response
The author does not provide a counter-argument but simply demands justification: 'Why do you say that?' He notes that the objector can find no ground.
This is a direct challenge to the premise.
3
Reflect on the logical point
Ask yourself: is it self-evident that divine creation invalidates accountability? The author shows that this connection is not proven.
The burden of proof lies with the objector.
4
Consider broader significance
This illustrates the importance of questioning assumed logical connections in theological arguments. Not all objections are valid without proof.
This method is central to kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : الدليل على أنه لا يجوز أن تكون أفعالنا خلقاً لله تعالى أنه لو كان هو الخالق لها، لم يصح أمره بها ونهيه عن بعضها وإثابته على الحسن الجميل منها وعقابه على القبيح من جملتها . قيل لهم : لم قلتم ذلك؟ فلا يجدون في ذلك وجهاً.
If they say: The proof that it is not permissible that our actions be created by God Almighty is that if He were the creator of them, His commanding them and prohibiting some of them and His rewarding for the good and beautiful among them and punishing for the ugly among them would not be valid. They are told: Why do you say that? So they find no ground in that.
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage presents another objection and a brief answer. The objector argues that if God were the creator of all actions, then His commands, prohibitions, rewards, and punishments would be meaningless because He would be commanding or prohibiting Himself. The author simply responds 'Why do you say that?'—challenging the objector to demonstrate the necessary connection between divine creation and the invalidity of moral accountability. Since they cannot provide a proof, the objection fails.

Key Terms
الأمر
النهي
الإثابة
العقاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God creates all actions, then commands, prohibitions, rewards, and punishments are automatically meaningless.
Correction: This is not self-evident; one must prove that divine creation logically negates moral accountability. The objector fails to provide such proof.

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the objection that divine creation would invalidate commands and rewards?
A) He provides a detailed proof
B) He simply asks 'Why do you say that?'
C) He accepts the objection
D) He redefines command

Key Takeaway
Not every objection is valid; even logical-sounding claims require proof, and the burden often lies with the one making the claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a theological truth because it seemed to contradict your assumptions, without actually examining whether the contradiction is necessary? Will you now commit to questioning your own objections with the same rigor the author demands of the objector?
?
When you face a difficult question about God's actions, do you tend to jump to a conclusion or do you pause and ask, 'Why do I think that?' How can you practice this discipline starting tomorrow?
?
The objector's claim sounded logical but had no ground. What claims do you hold about God that you have never critically examined? Will you take one hour this week to test them against revelation and sound reasoning?

God Commands Acquisition, Not Creation; Actions as Signs
Author offers alternative: God commands acquisition, not creation; or actions as signs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two alternative theological interpretations of divine command and human action: the acquisition model and the sign model.

Core Idea
God does not command creation—only acquisition, or actions are signs.

Imam al-Baqillani presents his opponents with two conceptual alternatives that safeguard God's exclusive creative power while explaining human accountability. Instead of requiring humans to create their acts, God may command acquisition (kasb) or treat actions as signs for reward and punishment. This passage challenges us to think differently about agency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic text and translation carefully. Notice the structure: the author presents two alternatives starting with 'ma anqartum an...'
The passage is one long rhetorical question.
2
Understand the first alternative
The first alternative: God commands and forbids acquisition (kasb), not creation (khalq), because creation is impossible for the servant.
Acquisition is a key Ash'ari concept.
3
Understand the second alternative
The second alternative: actions are merely signs (ʿilman) that God uses to decide reward and punishment, without needing commands and prohibitions.
This alternative even dispenses with commands.
4
Compare both alternatives
Reflect on how both alternatives uphold that God is the sole creator of actions, yet humans still have a role (acquisition or being the subject of signs).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال لهم : ما أنكرتم أن لا يكون الله تعالى آمراً لأحد من خلقه بخلق شيء من الأفعال ولا ناهياً له عن ذلك ولا مثيباً لأحد على أن خلق شيئاً ولا معاقباً له على ذلك، لأن الخلق مستحيل من العبد ؛ وأن يكون إنما أمر باكتساب ما خلقه ونهى عن ذلك وأثاب وعاقب وذمّ ومدح ووعد وتوعد على أن اكتسب العبد ما نهى عنه وأمر به فقط ؟ بل ما أنكرتم أن يكون إنما جعل هذه الأفعال علماً على إثابة من أحب إثابته وعقاب من أحب عقابه فقط ؟
Then they are told: What prevents you from [accepting] that God Almighty is not commanding anyone of His creation to create any of the actions, nor prohibiting him from that, nor rewarding anyone for creating anything, nor punishing him for that, because creation is impossible from the servant; and that He only commanded the acquisition of what He created and prohibited that, and rewarded and punished and praised and blamed and promised and threatened based on the servant acquiring what He prohibited and commanded only? Rather, what prevents you from [accepting] that He only made these actions a sign for rewarding whom He wills to reward and punishing whom He wills to punish only?
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage continues the refutation by presenting an alternative theological framework. The author suggests that God does not command or forbid the actual creation of actions, since creation is impossible for humans (only God creates). Instead, God commands and forbids the acquisition (kasb) of actions—the human dimension of involvement. Alternatively, the author proposes that actions are merely signs (علم) that God uses to decide who to reward or punish, without requiring that humans be the ultimate creators of those actions. This second option even dispenses with the need for commands and prohibitions.

Key Terms
الخلق
الاكتساب
العلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Commands and prohibitions require humans to create their actions.
Correction: The author argues that creation is impossible for humans; instead, God commands acquisition (kasb) or treats actions as signs.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is one possible purpose of actions if they are not created by humans?
A) Actions have no purpose
B) Actions are signs for reward and punishment
C) Actions are illusions
D) Actions are created by angels

Key Takeaway
God does not command humans to create actions; He commands acquisition or treats actions as signs, preserving divine sovereignty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that your every action is created by God alone, or do you sometimes feel you are the ultimate originator? How would your daily sense of accountability change if you internalized that you only acquire what God creates?
?
If your actions are signs of God's will for you, what do your current actions—especially the ones you hide—signal about your relationship with Him? Are you willing to let those signs speak honestly?
?
When you fall into sin, do you blame God for creating the act, or do you take responsibility for your acquisition? What would it cost you to stop excusing yourself and fully own your kasb before Him?

Objection from Evil: Are Evil Actions Impossible for God?
Objection from evil actions proves God is not creator; author asks for proof of necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classical kalam response to the problem of evil: the objector's claim that creating evil would make God evil, and the author's demand for proof of that necessary connection.

Core Idea
Creating evil does not automatically make the creator evil; the connection must be proven.

This passage presents a classic objection from the problem of evil: if God creates all actions, then He creates injustice and evil, which would make Him unjust. The objector claims this is impossible, so God cannot create human actions. But the author, Imam Baqillani, responds by questioning the necessity of that inference—why must creating evil make the creator evil? He demands a proof. This lesson unpacks that crucial logical move.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Identify the claim and the evidence given. The objector says that if God created evil actions, He would be unjust, oppressive, and foolish.
Notice the triple accusation: unjust, oppressive, foolish.
2
Focus on the author's response
The author does not deny the existence of evil; he questions why it logically follows that the creator must share the evil attribute of the action.
3
Reflect on the logical move
This response protects divine transcendence by requiring proof for the necessary connection between creating an evil act and being evil.
This is a typical kalam strategy: shift the burden of proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : الدليل على أن الله غير خالق لأفعال العباد أن منها الظلم والجور والفساد. فلو كان خالقاً لها، لكان بخلق الظلم والجور والسفه ظالماً جائراً سفيهاً . فلما لم يجز ذلك، صح ما قلناه . يقال لهم : لم قلتم إن هذا واجب، وما دليلكم عليه ؟
If they say: The proof that God is not the creator of the actions of servants is that among them are injustice, oppression, and corruption. For if He were the creator of them, then by creating injustice, oppression, and foolishness, He would be unjust, oppressive, and foolish. Since that is not permissible, what we have said is correct. They are told: Why do you say that this is necessary? And what is your proof for it?
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage presents the well-known 'problem of evil' objection. The objector argues that since God is just, He cannot be the creator of unjust, oppressive, or foolish actions. If He were, He would share those negative attributes. The author responds by questioning the necessity of that inference: 'Why do you say that this is necessary?' The author demands proof that creating evil necessarily makes the creator evil, rather than allowing for distinctions in agency.

Key Terms
الظلم
الجور
الفساد
السفه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming that if God creates an evil action, He must be evil himself.
Correction: The author argues that this connection is not self-evident; it requires proof. Creating an evil act does not automatically make the creator evil.

Checkpoint
What is the objector's main claim in this passage?
A) God creates only good actions
B) If God created evil actions, He would be evil
C) Evil actions do not exist
D) Humans create all actions

Key Takeaway
The classical kalam response to the problem of evil is to demand proof that creating evil necessarily makes the creator evil, preserving divine transcendence without denying the existence of evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What specific assumption about God are you tempted to make? This lesson challenges you to question whether that assumption is necessary. Will you examine it today?
?
The author asks, 'Why do you say this is necessary?'—a question that can be applied to many of your own beliefs about God. ?
Reflect on your own life: Have you ever judged a person's character solely by an action they performed, without considering their intention or context? How does that relate to the assumed connection between God creating evil and being evil? What does this teach you about how you judge others and how you think of God?

Distinction: Human Injustice vs Divine Creation for Others
Author distinguishes human injustice (for self, prohibited) from divine creation (for others).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why creating an action for others does not make the Creator unjust, based on the distinction between acting for oneself versus for others.

Core Idea
Injustice is blameworthy only when done for self, not when God creates it for others.

The objection that God cannot create injustice because that would make Him unjust is met with a precise distinction. The author clarifies that human injustice is blameworthy because it is done in oneself and for oneself, and against prohibition. God, however, creates these actions for others, not for Himself, so the blame does not apply.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Identify the objector's argument and the author's counter.
The objector argues from human analogy.
2
Focus on the key distinction
Notice the two phrases: 'fi nafsihi wa li nafsihi' (in himself and for himself) versus 'li ghayrihi' (for others). This is the core of the response.
3
Apply the distinction to the divine case
Understand that God's creation of injustice for others does not involve the blameworthy features of self-interest or disobedience, so God remains just.
This preserves divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : لأن فاعل الظلم منا ظالم وفاعل الجور منا جائر. قيل لهم : ما أنكرتم أن يكون فاعل الظلم والجور منا ظالماً جائراً لأنه منهي عنه وفاعل له في نفسه ولنفسه؟ والقديم تعالى يخلق الظلم والجور والسفه جوراً وظلماً وسفهاً لغيره لا لنفسه ولا في نفسه .
If they say: Because the doer of injustice among us is unjust and the doer of oppression among us is oppressive. They are told: What prevents you from [accepting] that the doer of injustice and oppression among us is unjust and oppressive because he is prohibited from it and he does it in himself and for himself? And the Eternal, Exalted, creates injustice, oppression, and foolishness as oppression, injustice, and foolishness for others, not for Himself and not in Himself.
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage continues the debate on evil. The objector insists that the human doer of injustice is called unjust, therefore God cannot create injustice. The author makes a crucial distinction: a human is unjust because he does the action in himself (fi nafsihi) and for himself (li nafsihi), and because he is prohibited from it. God, on the other hand, creates the same actions for others (li ghayrihi), not for Himself and not in Himself. Thus, the blameworthy aspect (being for oneself and against prohibition) is absent from God's creation, so He is not unjust.

Key Terms
الظلم
الجور
لنفسه
لغيره

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Creating an unjust action always makes the creator unjust.
Correction: The author shows that the moral status depends on whether the action is done for oneself or for others. God creates for others, so He is not unjust.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say a human doer of injustice is unjust?
A) Because he creates the action
B) Because he does it in himself and for himself, and is prohibited
C) Because he is powerful
D) Because God does not exist

Key Takeaway
God's creation of actions that are unjust for the recipient does not make Him unjust, because He does not do them for Himself or in Himself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a calamity or witness injustice in the world, do you ever feel that God is being unfair? How does this lesson's distinction challenge that feeling? Will you now consciously remind yourself that God's creation of trials is for your benefit, not for Himself? Commit to saying 'Allah does not wrong anyone' when such thoughts arise.
?
Are there times when you selfishly demand justice for yourself while ignoring the greater wisdom in others' suffering? How can you shift your perspective to see God's actions as for others (للغيره) rather than for self? Write down one specific situation where you will practice this shift.
?
If you truly internalize that God's actions are 'for others' and not for Himself, how would it change your reaction to personal hardship? Will you stop asking 'why me?' and start asking 'what is He teaching me?' What is one area of your life where you need to surrender to this truth?

Refuting the Qadarite Proof from 'And You Create Falsehood'
Baqillani refutes the Qadarite argument from Quranic verse on 'creating' falsehood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's linguistic refutation of the Qadarite argument that humans create their own actions based on the Quranic verse 'And you create falsehood'.

Core Idea
The word 'khalq' in the verse means fabrication, not creation.

The Qadarites (advocates of free will) often cited Quranic verses to support their claim that humans create their own actions. One such verse is {And you create falsehood}. In this passage, Imam al-Baqillani provides a clear linguistic refutation, showing that the term 'khalq' in this context means lying and fabrication, not bringing into existence. This lesson examines his argument and the evidence he adduces from the Quran and common usage.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once; then read the English translation. Pay attention to the structure: the Qadarite claim, the answer, and the supporting evidence.
Notice the use of the word 'khalq' in different contexts.
2
Identify the Qadarite argument
The Qadarites used the verse {And you create falsehood} to argue that humans create their actions. Locate this in the beginning of the passage.
3
Understand Baqillani's answer
Baqillani refutes by redefining 'khalq' as fabrication (takhalluq) and lies. He says the verse means 'you fabricate lies'.
This is a semantic argument: the word has multiple meanings.
4
Examine the supporting evidence
Baqillani provides two Quranic verses and a common expression to prove that 'khalq' can mean fabrication. Find them in the passage.
The verses are {This is nothing but fabrication} and {This is nothing but the lies of the ancients}.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this refutation undermines the Qadarite proof. The verse actually rebukes lying, not creating actions.
This shows the importance of accurate linguistic analysis in theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب ذكر آيات من القرآن. وإن احتجت القدرية في أن العباد يخلقون أفعالهم استدلوا بقوله تعالى: ﴿وتخلقون إفكاً﴾. فالجواب عنه أنه تعالى عنى: «إنكم تختلقون كذباً»، أي تتخرّصون وتكذبون كذباً. فالخلق يكون بمعنى الكذب والاختلاق. ومنه قوله تعالى: ﴿إن هذا إلا اختلاق﴾ و﴿إن هذا إلا خلق الأولين﴾ يعنون كذبهم. وقولهم «هذا حديث مخلوق» يريدون به هذا المعنى.
Chapter: Mention of Quranic verses. And if the Qadarites argue that the servants create their own actions, they adduced His statement: {And you create falsehood}. The answer for it is that He, may He be exalted, meant: 'You fabricate lies,' i.e., you guess and lie falsehood. So al-khalq can mean lying and fabrication. And from that is His statement: {This is nothing but fabrication} and {This is nothing but the lies of the ancients} meaning their lies. And their statement 'this is a fabricated hadith' they intend by it this meaning.
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage refutes the Qadarite argument based on the verse 'And you create falsehood' (al-Ankabut:17). The Qadarites claimed this verse proves humans create their own actions. Baqillani first states their argument, then provides the answer: the word 'khalq' (usually 'creation') here means lying and fabrication, not bringing into existence. He supports this linguistic point with two other Quranic verses where 'khalq' and its cognates appear in the sense of falsehood: 'This is nothing but fabrication' (Sad:7) and 'This is nothing but the lies of the ancients' (al-Shu'ara:137). He also cites the common expression 'this is a fabricated hadith' to show that 'makhluq' means 'fabricated'. Thus, the verse does not prove that humans create their actions; it rebukes them for inventing lies.

Key Terms
خلق
اختلاق
قدرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'khalq' in the Quran always means creating from nothing; thus the verse 'And you create falsehood' implies humans create their actions.
Correction: Baqillani shows that 'khalq' can also mean lying and fabrication, as evidenced by other Quranic verses and common Arabic usage.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what does 'khalq' mean in the verse 'And you create falsehood'?
A) To create from nothing
B) To fabricate lies
C) To form with the hand
D) To determine in the heart

Key Takeaway
Baqillani refutes the Qadarite proof by showing that the word 'khalq' in the verse means fabrication, not creation, thus the verse does not support human creation of actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever interpreted a Quranic verse in a way that fits your own desires or preconceived beliefs, just as the Qadarites did with 'And you create falsehood'? What steps can you take to ensure you submit to the intended meaning rather than twisting it?
?
When you read the Quran, do you prioritize isolated verses over the comprehensive understanding of the scholars, or do you actively seek the complete picture? This lesson shows how one verse can be misused if taken out of context. How will you guard against selective reading in your own study?
?
Are you willing to change a deeply held belief when you encounter clear evidence from the Quran and the scholarly tradition, even if it challenges your intellectual pride? The Qadarites clung to their doctrine despite the clear refutation. Is there any belief you hold too tightly?

Refuting the Qadarite Proof: The Meaning of 'Khalq' in Jesus Creating Birds
Baqillani explains that Jesus's 'creating' meant estimating and shaping, not true creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani refutes the Qadarite proof that humans can create, by distinguishing between the meanings of 'khalq'.

Core Idea
The word 'khalq' can mean estimation, not just creation from nothing.

The Qadarites claimed that Jesus's 'creating' birds from clay proves humans can create. Baqillani shows that 'khalq' in this verse means mental estimation and manual shaping, not bringing something from nonexistence into existence. This distinction preserves divine exclusivity over true creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let the author’s argument flow naturally.
Notice the two poetic examples at the end.
2
Identify the two meanings of 'khalq'
Baqillani says 'khalq' can mean estimation of the heart and shaping with the hand. Find these in the passage and note how they differ from ‘create from nothing’.
Record the Arabic terms for these meanings.
3
Examine the poetic evidence
Baqillani quotes two lines of poetry to support his interpretation. Read the translation and commentary to see how each line uses 'khalq' to mean determination or shaping.
How does this reinforce the point that the verse doesn't attribute true creation to Jesus?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن استدلوا بقوله عز وجل: ﴿وإذ تخلق من الطين كهيئة الطير بإذني﴾. فالمراد بذلك: إنك تُقدر بقلبك وتصوّر بيدك. والخلق يكون بمعنى تقدير القلب وفكرته ويكون معناه تصوير اليد وحركاتها واعتماداتها التي يخلق عنده أشكال ما ماسته اليد وباشرته. ونحن لا ننكر أن يكون عيسى عليه السلام مفكراً بقلبه ومحركاً ليده وجوارحه حركات وفكراً يخلق الله عنده اجتماع المصدورات من الأجسام. قال الشاعر زهير: ولأنت تَفْري مَا خَلَقْتَ وَبَعْضُ الْقَوْم يَخْلُقُ ثُمَّ لَا يَفْرِي. يعني تمضي ما قدرت ومنهم من يقدر ثم لا يمضي. وقال آخر: وَلا يُنيطُ بِأَيْدِي الْخَالِقِينَ وَلا أَيْدِي الْخَوَالِقِ إِلَّا جَيْدَ الأَدَمِ. يريد أيدي المقدرين للأدم بأيديهم وقلوبهم.
And if they adduced His statement, Mighty and Majestic: {And when you create from clay like the shape of a bird with My permission}. The intended meaning of that is: 'You determine in your heart and form with your hand.' And al-khalq can mean the estimation of the heart and its thought, and it can mean the shaping of the hand and its movements and pressures by which He creates the forms of what the hand has touched and handled. And we do not deny that Jesus, peace be upon him, was thinking with his heart and moving his hand and limbs with movements and thought at which Allah creates the gathering of the shaped things from bodies. The poet Zuhayr said: 'And you cut what you created, while some people create and then do not cut.' Meaning: you execute what you determined, and among them are those who determine and then do not execute. And another said: 'And no one attaches, by the hands of creators nor by the hands of makers, except the neck of leather.' He intends: the hands of those who determine the leather with their hands and hearts.
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage refutes the Qadarite argument from the verse where Jesus 'creates' birds from clay (al-Ma'ida:110). The Qadarites claimed this proves humans can create. Baqillani's answer is that 'khalq' here means estimation in the heart and shaping with the hand, not creation ex nihilo. Jesus's actions are merely physical movements and mental processes at which Allah creates the resulting forms. Baqillani acknowledges that Jesus indeed thought and moved his hands, but the actual creation is by Allah. He supports this interpretation with two lines of Arabic poetry: Zuhayr's line uses 'khalaqta' to mean 'you determined' (followed by 'tafri' meaning to execute), and another poet uses 'khaliqin' to mean 'those who determine' (as in estimating leather). Thus, the verse does not attribute creative power to humans.

Key Terms
خلق
تقدير
قدرية
مصدورات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the word 'khalq' always means bringing something from nonexistence into existence, and therefore Jesus created the birds independently.
Correction: Baqillani shows that 'khalq' can mean estimation and shaping, and true creation is by Allah alone.

Checkpoint
What does Baqillani say 'khalq' means in the verse about Jesus creating birds?
A) To bring into existence from nothing
B) To estimate in the heart and shape with the hand
C) To fabricate lies
D) To command into existence

Key Takeaway
Baqillani shows that the word 'khalq' in the verse about Jesus refers to planning and shaping, not creating from nothing, thus nullifying the Qadarite proof.

Do you truly believe that your plans only materialize by God's creation? Refuting the Qadarite Proof from 'Best of Creators'
Baqillani interprets 'best of creators' as metaphorical or as meaning 'best of estimators'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Baqillani interprets the Qur'anic phrase 'best of creators' to refute the Qadarite claim that humans create their own actions.

Core Idea
The verse 'best of creators' does not prove human creation; it is metaphorical.

The Qadarites (early free-will theologians) often cited the Qur'anic verse 'Blessed is Allah, the best of creators' (23:14) to argue that humans are genuine creators of their deeds, since the verse compares God to other creators. In this passage, Imam Baqillani dismantles that argument by offering two distinct interpretations of the verse, both of which preserve God's exclusive creative power. Let's examine his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Note the two main answers Baqillani gives.
The passage is concise; read it twice.
2
Understand the first answer: 'best of estimators'
Baqillani first explains that the verse means 'best of those who estimate and form.' Humans do estimate and form, but God's estimation and forming are superior.
This interprets 'khalq' (creation) as 'taqdir' (estimation) and 'taswir' (forming).
3
Understand the second answer: metaphorical usage
Baqillani then provides a second possibility: the term 'creators' is used metaphorically, as in Arabic idioms where non-creators are called creators by extension.
He gives examples from Arabic: 'the two just ones' for Abu Bakr and Umar, 'the two black ones' for water and dates, etc.
4
Connect to the Qadarite misconception
Identify the common misunderstanding: that the phrase implies multiple true creators. Both answers show that the verse does not attribute creative power to humans.
The lesson guards against taking the verse literally without context.
5
Test your understanding with the checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question to verify you can identify Baqillani's explanations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن استدلوا بقوله تعالى: ﴿فتبارك الله أحسن الخالقين﴾. فالجواب عنه أنه تعالى عنى وهو أعلم أحسن المقدرين تقديراً وأحسن المصوّرين تصويراً. يقول إن تصويره ألطف وأحسن من تصويرهم وإن تقديره التي هي إرادته وقصده أصوب من تقديرهم وارتيائهم. ويحتمل أن يكون الله تعالى لما ذكر نفسه مع غيره الذي ليس بخالق، سماهم باسمه مجازاً واتساعاً. كما قالوا «عدل العمرين» يعنون أبا بكر وعمر. وكما قالوا «الأسودان» يعنون الماء والتمر. وكما قال الشاعر: أَخَذْنَا بِآفَاقِ السَّمَاءِ عَلَيْكُمُ لَنَا قَمَرَاهَا وَالنُّجُومُ الطَّوَالِعُ. يعني الشمس والقمر. فكذلك قوله ﴿الخالقين﴾، والخالق منهم واحد.
And if they adduced His statement: {So blessed is Allah, the best of creators}. The answer for it is that He, may He be exalted, meant—and He knows best—the best of those who estimate in estimation and the best of those who form in forming. He says: His forming is more subtle and better than their forming, and His estimation, which is His will and intention, is more correct than their estimation and deliberation. And it is possible that when Allah mentioned Himself together with others who are not creators, He called them by His name metaphorically and broadly. Just as they said 'the two just ones of the two 'Umars' meaning Abu Bakr and 'Umar. And just as they said 'the two black ones' meaning water and dates. And just as the poet said: 'We took by the horizons of the sky over you, for us its two moons and the rising stars,' meaning the sun and the moon. So likewise His statement {the creators}, while the Creator among them is One.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage refutes the Qadarite argument from the verse 'Blessed is Allah, the best of creators' (al-Mu'minun:14). The Qadarites argued that the comparison 'best of creators' implies that there are other creators besides Allah. Baqillani gives two answers. First, the verse means 'the best of those who estimate' (أحسن المقدرين) and 'the best of those who form' (أحسن المصوّرين), i.e., in terms of estimation and shaping, which humans also do, but Allah's estimation and forming are superior. Second, it is possible that Allah calls others 'creators' in a metaphorical (مجاز) and broad (اتساع) sense, as in Arabic idiom where 'al- aswadan' (the two black ones) means water and dates, and 'qamaraha' (its two moons) means the sun and moon. Thus, the verse does not affirm any true creative power to humans.

Key Terms
خلق
مجاز
اتساع
قدرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the phrase 'best of creators' proves that humans are true creators of their actions.
Correction: Baqillani shows that the verse either refers to estimation/forming (not creation ex nihilo) or uses 'creators' metaphorically, so no real creative power is attributed to humans.

Checkpoint
What is one explanation Baqillani gives for the phrase 'best of creators' in the Quran?
A) It proves humans can create
B) It is metaphorical, calling non-creators 'creators'
C) It refers to angels
D) It was abrogated

Key Takeaway
Baqillani refutes the Qadarite proof by showing that 'best of creators' either means 'best of estimators' or uses 'creator' metaphorically, thus denying any real creative power to humans.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse like 'best of creators', do you immediately assume it supports your existing view, or do you pause to consider how scholars have understood it? This lesson shows how a single phrase can be twisted. In what area of your faith are you most vulnerable to reading your own desires into the Qur'an?
?
Baqillani used linguistic examples to show that the plural 'creators' does not imply multiple true creators. Are there passages in the Qur'an or hadith that you interpret literally, even though a metaphorical reading might be more consistent with Islamic theology? How willing are you to let go of a comfortable interpretation when the evidence points otherwise?
?
The Qadarites built an entire doctrine on one phrase. Reflect: Is there a belief you hold that rests on a single verse or hadith, while ignoring broader Islamic teachings? What would it cost you to re-examine that belief in light of the consensus of scholars like Baqillani?

Interpreting Obedience in Quran 4:64
Baqillani explains the meaning of obedience in Quran 4:64.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's two interpretative approaches to reconciling the universal command to obey messengers with divine foreknowledge of who will actually obey.

Core Idea
Obedience to messengers is known by God, not forced by the verse.

This passage addresses a theological puzzle: if God sends messengers to be obeyed, why do some people disobey? Baqillani offers two nuanced interpretations that preserve both God's knowledge and human responsibility. The first links obedience to divine foreknowledge; the second reframes the verse as a command to obey, not a guarantee of outcome.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Start by reading the full passage in Arabic and English. Notice the structure: an objection is posed, then two possible interpretations are given.
The verse in question is Quran 4:64.
2
Identify the two interpretations
The first interpretation specifies that obedience is meant only from those God knows will obey. The second reinterprets 'to be obeyed' as 'to command obedience'. Compare how each handles the apparent universal scope of the verse.
3
Reflect on theological implications
Consider how each interpretation upholds divine foreknowledge (first) and the universal command (second) while avoiding the implication that God forces obedience or that His will is thwarted.
This connects to broader discussions of qadar and human free will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة
فإن قالوا : فما معنى قوله : وما أرسلنا من رسول إلا ليطاع بإذن الله ؟ .
قيل لهم : معنى ذلك : إلا ليطيعه من في المعلوم أنه يطيع وينقاد دون من علم أنه لا يطيع ولا ينقاد. ويمكن أن يكون التأويل في ذلك : إلا ليأمر بطاعته والانقياد له فيكون معنى «إِلَّا لِيُطَاعَ»: إلا ليأمر المكلفين بطاعته جميعاً لتقع الطاعة من جميعهم.
A question: If they say: What is the meaning of His statement: 'And We did not send any messenger except to be obeyed by permission of Allah'? It is said to them: The meaning of that is: except that he be obeyed by the one who, in [divine] knowledge, will obey and submit, excluding the one whom He knows will not obey nor submit. And it is possible that the interpretation in that is: except to command obedience to him and submission to him, so the meaning of 'except to be obeyed' is: except to command the morally obligated to obey him altogether, so that obedience occurs from all of them.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage presents a theological objection-and-answer regarding the Qur'anic verse 'And We did not send any messenger except to be obeyed by permission of Allah' (Quran 4:64). The objectors ask about its meaning, presumably because the verse seems to imply that all people were sent messengers in order to obey them, yet many disbelieve. The answer gives two possible interpretations. The first interpretation specifies that the obedience meant is from those whom God knows in His eternal knowledge will obey and submit, excluding those He knows will not obey. This preserves divine foreknowledge and predestination. The second interpretation rephrases the verse to mean that the purpose of sending messengers is to command obedience, i.e., the command is universal, but the actual obedience may or may not occur from individuals. Both interpretations reconcile the divine will with human free will and God's knowledge of the outcomes.

Key Terms
الطاعة
المعلوم
التأويل
المكلفين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the verse implies God forces all people to obey the messenger, making disbelief contradictory.
Correction: Baqillani clarifies that the verse refers either to those whom God knows will obey (not all people) or to the command to obey (not the actual occurrence of obedience).

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani's first interpretation of Quran 4:64, who is meant by 'to be obeyed'?
A) All people without exception
B) Only those who God knows will obey
C) Only the Prophet
D) Only the angels

Key Takeaway
Baqillani shows that divine foreknowledge of obedience does not negate the universal command to obey; the verse can be understood either as specifying those who will obey or as commanding all to obey.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
